### 19. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Introduction--Stock-Based-Compensation

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Introduction--Stock-Based-Compensation/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Introduction--Stock-Based-Compensation.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Introduction--Stock-Based-Compensation\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Introduction--Stock-Based-Compensation.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] We're going to start with stock based compensation expense.
[00:04 - 00:08] Most companies that issue stock options to employees issue them at the money and with
[00:08 - 00:13] vesting periods meaning that employees are unable to immediately derive value from options.
[00:13 - 00:19] However, stock options that are issued at the money and have a vesting period are still
[00:19 - 00:21] better than not having anything.
[00:21 - 00:26] Most employees would certainly prefer to have at the money options with a vesting period
[00:26 - 00:29] over not having them and that means that they clearly have some value.
[00:29 - 00:32] The value is just derived in what's going to happen in the future.
[00:32 - 00:37] And so because of that, under gap, companies are required to estimate this value today,
[00:37 - 00:42] that's the accrual principle and recognize it as compensation expense.
[00:42 - 00:44] Just like regular wages.
[00:44 - 00:49] Now companies can use a variety of option pricing models to estimate this value.
[00:49 - 00:54] But unlike salary, the company doesn't have to issue cash immediately or even stock
[00:54 - 00:55] immediately.
[00:55 - 01:00] The expense recognized on the income statement is therefore a non cash expense like depreciation.
[01:00 - 01:10] It represents simply a accounting valuation of the potential future value of stock options.
[01:10 - 01:14] Despite the gap requirement though, most analysts and investors covering industries where
[01:14 - 01:20] there is a lot of stock based compensation generally ignore the expense and adjust reported
[01:20 - 01:22] earnings to exclude the expense.
[01:22 - 01:26] Just do that under the argument that it is a non cash expense.
[01:26 - 01:30] It's based on a whole bunch of assumptions about what's going to happen in the future.
[01:30 - 01:36] And it's therefore the argument is made better to just ignore it altogether and presumably
[01:36 - 01:40] in industries where there's a similar amount of stock based compensation awards as a part
[01:40 - 01:41] of the total package.
[01:41 - 01:47] If you ignore all of it, you remove some noise from the income statement that is non cash
[01:47 - 01:50] and it makes analyzing companies across the same industry a little bit easier.
[01:50 - 01:54] That's the argument for ignoring this gap requirement basically.
[01:54 - 02:00] Now since stock based compensation expense reduces gap tax expense, this is another nuance
[02:00 - 02:04] of what happens when you ignore stock based compensation expense as most analysts do.
[02:04 - 02:09] What ends up happening is because stock based compensation expense reduces gap taxes when
[02:09 - 02:14] analysts ignore stock based compensation expense, they should also ignore the reduction to
[02:14 - 02:17] gap tax expense due to the stock based compensation.
[02:17 - 02:21] If you're going to pretend like you don't have the company that your analyzer doesn't
[02:21 - 02:26] have stock based compensation expense, you should also pretend like the company doesn't
[02:26 - 02:32] get a tax benefit from having this expense.
[02:32 - 02:36] Like depreciation and remortization, stock based compensation is not explicitly identified
[02:36 - 02:41] on the income statement, but because it is a non cash item, it's added back to net
[02:41 - 02:46] income to get to cash from operations on the cash flow statement and you see that here.
[02:46 - 02:48] Here is Apple's cash flow statement.
[02:48 - 02:52] It starts with net income which is the last line on the income statement and the second
[02:52 - 02:57] add back after depreciation amortization is stock based compensation which is presumably buried
[02:57 - 03:01] within the operating line items on the income statement and is a non cash item that needs
[03:01 - 03:03] to be added back.
[03:03 - 03:08] For Apple, like many companies that issue significant amounts of stock as part of employee
[03:08 - 03:16] compensation, this line item can be a significant non cash component of expenses.
[03:16 - 03:21] Once we turn to amortization and the treatment by analysts of amortization expense and
[03:21 - 03:28] how it's different from gap, so amortization as we know is a non cash expense, but it is
[03:28 - 03:34] sometimes ignored by analysts when analyzing earnings, meaning the reported gap based results
[03:34 - 03:40] for a company's income statement are adjusted by analysts to exclude the impact of this
[03:40 - 03:41] amortization.
[03:41 - 03:47] When amortization is ignored, the earnings per share without the amortization expense is
[03:47 - 03:49] often called cash earnings per share.
[03:49 - 03:56] The logic of removing amortization is similar to the logic of removing stock based compensation
[03:56 - 04:02] in the sense that both are non cash expenses and they're subject to assumptions, useful
[04:02 - 04:07] life assumptions that the management puts on the intangible assets that generate the
[04:07 - 04:12] amortization and therefore is a way to sort of wash away those subjective accounting
[04:12 - 04:14] adjustments that have no cash impact.
[04:14 - 04:18] This is obviously a violation of some basic tenets of accrual accounting.
[04:18 - 04:23] One of the counter arguments is that for companies that are inquisitive, they make a lot of
[04:23 - 04:29] acquisitions and are buying up a lot of intangible assets that generate a lot of amortization,
[04:29 - 04:36] the large amortization expenses can really skew the picture of true operating performance.
[04:36 - 04:42] This being an accounting driven and assumption driven expense, imagine a company that has
[04:42 - 04:46] bought a whole boatload of intangible assets and comparing it to another company that hasn't
[04:46 - 04:48] just bought a whole boatload of intangible assets.
[04:48 - 04:53] The company that just made those acquisitions is going to have massive amortization expenses
[04:53 - 04:56] that's going to make it difficult to actually fair it out what's happening from a core
[04:56 - 05:01] operational standpoint that doesn't have to do with these large acquisitions.
[05:01 - 05:05] That's the justification of the argument for ignoring amortization expense.

---

[00:00 - 00:05] So there's a point that you get to in the comps analysis where you finish in putting all of the

[03:00 - 03:06] You then should not count it in the net debt calculation and that just creates and that's consistent logic

[03:11 - 03:16] We're already capturing an equity value that assumes conversion from all the convert shareholders
[03:16 - 03:23] We should not be adding them. We should not be removing them yet a second time from equity value to get to enterprise value
[03:23 - 03:30] Not controlling interest represent the portion of the consolidated business that common shareholders do not own and therefore
[03:30 - 03:34] The value of the non-controlling interest should be removed from
[03:35 - 03:39] Enterprise value to get to equity value advice versus should be added to equity value to get to enterprise value
[03:39 - 03:44] And we use the book value as a proxy. It's not always the right the right number
[03:44 - 03:50] But we use the book value in practice almost always as a proxy because we usually it's very difficult to find a market values for

### 37. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Non-GAAP-Presentation-on-Financial-Statements

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Non-GAAP-Presentation-on-Financial-Statements/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Non-GAAP-Presentation-on-Financial-Statements.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Non-GAAP-Presentation-on-Financial-Statements\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Non-GAAP-Presentation-on-Financial-Statements.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:01 - 00:11] So the adjustments discussed so far, stock-based compensation, other non-cash items like amortization expense, and non-recurring items are so common for some industries.
[00:11 - 00:22] The companies increasingly align their presentations and disclosures to facilitate this analysis that we've been talking about, basically ignoring these items.
[00:22 - 00:28] Now, companies are not allowed to deviate from gap-based presentation in the core financial statements.
[00:28 - 00:33] You can't just exclude these items from your income statement. Gap doesn't allow you to do that.
[00:33 - 00:40] But what companies can do is provide additional non-gap schedules and provide non-gap future guidance.
[00:40 - 00:51] In fact, management guides are typically presented on a non-gap basis with guidance for expenses and earnings per share, excluding things like stock-based compensation and unusual or frequent items.
[00:52 - 01:07] And in parallel, analysts consensus earnings per share estimates. When you try to understand what is the analyst community's consensus EPS expectations for a business, those EPS consensus estimates are usually communicated on a non-gap basis.
[01:07 - 01:15] The quote-unquote, real numbers that analysts are thinking about are the numbers that have been adjusted from the gap figures to the non-gap figures.
[01:15 - 01:21] And quite often, specific industries have their own additional and unique non-gap conventions.
[01:21 - 01:40] For example, the real estate industry looks at non-gap conventions in terms of how to calculate profit and things of that nature, other industries, whether it's financial institutions or oil and gas businesses, all have their own additional and unique non-gap types of conventions.
[01:40 - 01:50] Now, one of the challenges with these non-gap presentations is that there is no uniform presentation of these items on the income statement.
[01:50 - 01:57] Press releases and conference call transcripts are usually the best places to look for these non-gap presentations.
[01:57 - 02:05] Management often provides analyst-friendly presentations and disclosures in both of these platforms when they're communicating their quarterly results.
[02:05 - 02:21] But also in the 10K and 10Q, there are often aggregations of non-gap data within major expense categories like cost of goods sold, SGA operating expenses, and that may or may not be disclosed in the footnotes.
[02:21 - 02:30] So, in terms of where to find these non-gap presentations, probably the most likely places, since they're not required, there's not a place you're always going to find it.
[02:30 - 02:48] But most of the time it's going to be in the press release, there's going to be some more color provided in the associated conference call transcript, and then in the 10K and 10Q, there will likely be disclosures if they're explicitly communicated in the press release they're most likely going to also be communicated, but not always in the 10K and 10Q.
[02:48 - 02:52] Remember, the difference here is you're not required to communicate anything non-gap.
[02:52 - 03:08] So, here we have a company called VMware that has provided as part of their year-end press release, a full reconciliation of what their gap-based income statement looks like.
[03:08 - 03:14] And this is, you see, they have revenues of $5 billion operating income of $1 billion.
[03:14 - 03:25] And you see that they're making a whole bunch of adjustments, these columns between gap and non-gap are a whole bunch of adjustments to the gap-based financials to get to what non-gap looks like.
[03:25 - 03:36] So, before we go into some of these line items, let's actually take a step back and see that gap-based revenues of $5.2 billion are the same as non-gap-based revenues.
[03:36 - 03:45] It's actually quite rare that there are going to be non-gap adjustments to revenue. Everything we've been talking about is the exclusion of certain expenses and certain non-operating income items.
[03:45 - 03:50] So, this is pretty consistent with what you typically see that there's not going to be any adjustments on the revenue side.
[03:50 - 03:59] But once you get to the expense side, let's take a look at just cost of goods sold, for example, cost of license revenues and cost of service revenues.
[03:59 - 04:13] While the company reported gap of together something around $730 million, there's a whole bunch of adjustments in down from $210 and $5.20 to $84.48 associated with various activities.
[04:13 - 04:23] So, one activity's stock-based compensation. Notice this company excludes stock-based compensation expense from all of the line items where stock-based compensation expenses recognize.
[04:23 - 04:31] But then there's several other adjustments. Intangible asset-semitization. This is that non-cash-semitization expense we talked about.
[04:31 - 04:35] Was buried within cost of goods sold as being excluded.
[04:35 - 04:39] And then you have several other adjustments here that are specific to VMware.
[04:39 - 04:44] We talked about some of the most common ones and here you see them being reflected.
[04:44 - 04:50] If you scroll down, if we go to the next slide, you'll see that moving beyond operating income all the way down in that income,
[04:50 - 04:56] there's a whole bunch of other adjustments, for example, other income. So, the company reported a gap-based other income of $28.
[04:56 - 05:06] Well, actually, virtually all of that is, and it considered an unusual activity because it's tied to the gain on the disposition of an asset.
[05:06 - 05:12] It's considered a gain on the sale of an asset and from a non-gap perspective, we don't want to look at gains.
[05:12 - 05:17] And so, the non-gap adjustment is actually a loss. We're actually looking at an other loss.
[05:17 - 05:26] So, it changes the resulting profitability. So, if you look at this company, they had income before taxes on a gap basis of $1.1 million.
[05:26 - 05:31] Sorry, $1.1 billion. It's actually $1.8 billion, far better on a non-gap basis.
[05:31 - 05:44] And that's pretty common. Actually, management often will present their non-gap financials in a way where the net result is that operations that their operating profit is much higher than it would have been on a gap basis.
[05:44 - 05:53] Companies are much more likely to want to highlight non-recurring expenses than they would like to highlight non-recurring income.
[05:53 - 05:58] And there's quite a bit of subjectivity about what you choose to identify as non-recurring.
[05:58 - 06:05] So, the last adjustment here is income tax provision on a gap basis is $133 million.
[06:05 - 06:15] But, because we've excluded so many expenses, we need to actually bump up taxes to what it would have been had there been much fewer expenses.
[06:15 - 06:25] So, that's the catch-20 too. If you're going to basically exclude what looks like between gap income before taxes of $1.1 billion and non-gap $1.8 billion,
[06:25 - 06:32] that's about $650 million worth of expenses, net expenses that the company is ignoring.
[06:32 - 06:40] Most of them notice just coming from stock-based compensation. There's about $460 million worth of stock-based compensation that the company is ignoring.
[06:40 - 06:41] That's the big driver of this.
[06:41 - 06:50] Well, if you're going to pretend like you don't have stock-based compensation expense, you should pretend like the tax shield associated with that as well as intangible.
[06:50 - 06:53] If you don't have an organization, some of these other items doesn't exist either.
[06:53 - 07:05] And notice that the net result is, while on a gap basis, diluted EPS is $2.34 on a non-gap basis, it's significantly higher, $3.37.
[07:05 - 07:17] And that is the essence of why this becomes so important. The way that analysts and companies communicate about the performance of the company under question is very much on a non-gap basis.
[07:17 - 07:33] And so understanding these adjustments is crucial to understanding why it is when you pick up a company's 10K or 10Q, the EPS that you see in the operating profit you see is very different from the actual operating profit and the EPS and all the metrics that the analysts and investment community is talking about.
[07:33 - 07:35] It's because of these adjustments.

### 38. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Nonrecurring-Items-Overview

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Nonrecurring-Items-Overview/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Nonrecurring-Items-Overview.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Nonrecurring-Items-Overview\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Nonrecurring-Items-Overview.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:01 - 00:06] Last, we turned to the most interesting of these three most common
[00:06 - 00:10] non-gap adjustments. And that is non-recurring items.
[00:10 - 00:14] Non-recurring items are really a cluster of a variety of types of activities
[00:14 - 00:18] which analysts exclude from the income statement.
[00:18 - 00:22] So there are actually three broad types of activities
[00:22 - 00:28] that even GAAP wants you to basically present separately from the rest of the income statement.
[00:28 - 00:35] Those three types of activities are discontinued operations, extraordinary items, and accounting changes.
[00:35 - 00:44] Discontinued operations are gains and losses from operations that a company has discontinued.
[00:44 - 00:48] When a company discontinues or sells off a distinct part of its business,
[00:48 - 00:54] the income and expense from this part of the business, as well as severance, facility closure expenses,
[00:54 - 00:58] all will be recognized below net income in a separate category.
[00:58 - 01:07] So if there is any revenue during a particular year from an operation that is all the sudden now deemed to be discontinued
[01:07 - 01:13] and will presumably be sold down the road, that revenue, the associated expenses,
[01:13 - 01:16] all of that are actually separated out of the main income statement
[01:16 - 01:23] and recognizes just one net line, net loss or gain from those discontinued operations below net income.
[01:23 - 01:29] And that's the same from a GAAP presentation as well as from an analyst presentation.
[01:29 - 01:33] The discontinued operations are generally treated separately from the income statement,
[01:33 - 01:40] causing no problems for us in terms of analyzing earnings per share or profitability or things like that.
[01:40 - 01:42] Same goes for extraordinary items.
[01:42 - 01:47] These are things like losses from the expropriation of assets or accounting changes.
[01:47 - 01:52] So for example, if a company changes its accounting method for depreciation or inventory accounting,
[01:52 - 01:58] they switch from life out of FIFO, the corresponding expenses on the income statement would change.
[01:58 - 02:04] Under GAAP, any impact on the income statement,
[02:04 - 02:10] on expenses associated with changes in accounting and accounting rules or assumptions,
[02:10 - 02:14] those changes have to be identified separately below the income statement,
[02:14 - 02:19] making it easy to compare this year's income statement to the prior year income statement
[02:19 - 02:25] statement without the noise of those changes which do impact the year over year comparisons.
[02:25 - 02:30] For the above activities, the three that we just talked about discontinued operations, extraordinary items,
[02:30 - 02:35] and accounting changes, FASB and analysts are generally in agreement.
[02:35 - 02:40] As long as we report the net of taxes as a separate line below net income,
[02:40 - 02:44] it doesn't muddy the waters of ongoing profitability.
[02:44 - 02:47] And where I'm going with this is that there are some activities
[02:47 - 02:50] where FASB and analysts are in disagreement.
[02:50 - 02:57] Before we get into line items where FASB and analysts are generally in disagreement,
[02:57 - 03:02] where the GAAP base presentation is different from how analysts want to see these items.
[03:02 - 03:06] Let's look at what Walmart's income statement looks like.
[03:06 - 03:11] And we see that Walmart, in line with the gap rules that we just talked about for discontinued operations,
[03:11 - 03:20] identifies that the net gain or loss from operations that they deem to be discontinued
[03:20 - 03:24] is presented distinctly from the rest of their continuing operations.
[03:24 - 03:31] So we have Walmart's revenue, costs of goods sold, interest expense, taxes,
[03:31 - 03:33] all the way down to continuing operations.
[03:33 - 03:38] Notice this line item called income from loss of continued operations,
[03:38 - 03:43] from discontinued operations, represents a net aggregation of revenues,
[03:43 - 03:48] net of expenses for entities that are no longer lumped into revenues and expenses here.
[03:48 - 03:54] And so the reason why analysts and FASB is generally in agreement about the right treatment for this
[03:54 - 03:58] is analysts don't want to look at the noise,
[03:58 - 04:02] the other option was to identify the income and loss from discontinued operations
[04:02 - 04:06] and bury it within these line-ups, to have revenues be a little bit higher
[04:06 - 04:09] presumably because discontinued operations were generating revenue.
[04:09 - 04:12] And now I've costing good costs and expenses a little bit higher
[04:12 - 04:15] because they're discontinued operations with some expenses.
[04:15 - 04:18] But rather take all that out and just net it as a separate line item.
[04:18 - 04:20] That's what FASB wants to do and that's what analysts want to do.
[04:20 - 04:22] They want to look at core ongoing profitability.
[04:22 - 04:26] And basically you look at this as something that's already done that's not going to be recurring in the future.
[04:26 - 04:30] Because next year these operations are going to be presumably discontinued.

[09:20 - 09:22] So we only need to adjust the operating items,
[09:22 - 09:25] the exclusions to the operating items.

### 45. Trading-Comps-Modeling-PE--PEG-Ratios

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-PE--PEG-Ratios/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-PE--PEG-Ratios.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-PE--PEG-Ratios\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-PE--PEG-Ratios.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:07] In the next several slides, we are going to go through and define and highlight some issues with the most common multiples.
[00:07 - 00:13] Now, when you spread carbs, when you perform a comps analysis or when you perform any sort of valuation,
[00:13 - 00:17] what you'll recognize is that there are some ubiquitous multiples out there.
[00:17 - 00:21] EV to EBITDA is a multiple that's used across a wide array of industries,
[00:21 - 00:24] and the same goes for the PE ratio that we're going to introduce here.
[00:24 - 00:27] Other multiples are fairly industry-specific.
[00:27 - 00:33] Determining the multiple that's appropriate to use in your analysis is going to be a function of the industry that the company is in,
[00:33 - 00:35] and the specific state of the company.
[00:35 - 00:42] So, these are all decisions that you become more and more comfortable with and more you understand the business and the more you understand the industry.
[00:42 - 00:47] And what we're striving to do over the next several slides is just give you a sense of which industries are appropriate for which multiples.
[00:47 - 00:52] So, the PE ratio, probably the most common next to the EV to EBITDA multiple,
[00:52 - 00:54] probably the two most common multiples out there.
[00:54 - 01:00] There are two ways to actually define the PE ratio. One is to define it as price per share divided by earnings per share.
[01:00 - 01:04] The other way to define it is equity value divided by net income.
[01:04 - 01:11] Both ratios are actually considered interchangeable typically as price per share is really just equity value per share,
[01:11 - 01:15] whereas EPS is really just net income per share.
[01:15 - 01:21] Now, the one caveat to that is when you're trying to get to equity value from share price,
[01:21 - 01:24] you want to multiply by the latest shares outstanding,
[01:24 - 01:28] whereas to get from earnings per share to net income,
[01:28 - 01:31] you actually want to multiply by weighted average shares outstanding,
[01:31 - 01:37] because recall that EPS is defined as net income divided by weighted average shares outstanding,
[01:37 - 01:39] not latest shares outstanding.
[01:39 - 01:44] So, the shares that you're multiplying by is different from the numerator and the denominator,
[01:44 - 01:48] and that could lead to a slight difference, usually it's not a material difference,
[01:48 - 01:54] but it could lead to a slight difference between the PE ratio that's calculated using share price DPS,
[01:54 - 01:58] versus the PE ratio that's defined as equity value divided by net income.
[01:58 - 02:05] So, just be aware of what the numerator and the denominator is specifically when you're using the PE ratio,
[02:05 - 02:09] and understand that there is this subtle difference.
[02:09 - 02:15] The difference only becomes material if there are massive changes to the share count during the year.
[02:15 - 02:19] If, for example, during the year, the company went on a massive buyback,
[02:19 - 02:26] where the company issued a lot of shares, those are scenarios in which the PE ratio calculated using the share price DPS approach
[02:26 - 02:30] might be materiality different than equity value, net income approach.
[02:30 - 02:35] So, beyond this sort of granular difference in terms of calculation,
[02:35 - 02:39] let's understand what the point of the PE ratio is,
[02:39 - 02:46] and the point is really that the EPS is used as a proxy for economic equity value.
[02:46 - 02:54] Now, the problem with using EPS as a proxy for value is that EPS is a measure of accounting profit only during a particular period of time.
[02:54 - 02:59] We know from our basic understanding of corporate finance that the value of a business,
[02:59 - 03:03] that the value of a share shouldn't be an exact function of its earnings per share,
[03:03 - 03:06] but it should be a function of all that's expected future cash flows,
[03:06 - 03:11] discounted to the present at some rate that appropriately reflects the riskiness of the cash flows.
[03:11 - 03:14] So, the challenges, of course, EPS is just in a measure of accounting profit.
[03:14 - 03:19] It can be significantly divorced from the cash flows that the business generates,
[03:19 - 03:24] and measures only one particular period of time if you're using last 12 months EPS.
[03:24 - 03:27] That doesn't necessarily mean that future EPS is going to be similar.
[03:27 - 03:30] This year can be very different from EPS next year or the year after that.
[03:30 - 03:34] So, accounting profits can be misleading because they include non-cash and non-recurring items,
[03:34 - 03:37] as well as accounting assumptions that are embedded within EPS,
[03:37 - 03:40] such as historical versus market costing.
[03:40 - 03:43] And this means that EPS can be goose, it can be manipulated.
[03:43 - 03:50] Also, a high-pea valuation relative to Pierce could be justified when high-pea firm has higher growth prospects.
[03:50 - 03:53] So, when you're comparing companies based on a PE ratio and you say,
[03:53 - 03:58] oh, my company has a low PE ratio compared to its peers that are trading at a high-pea ratio.
[03:58 - 04:00] Therefore, my company is undervalued.
[04:00 - 04:04] Well, that may not necessarily be true for slew of reasons.
[04:04 - 04:11] Not the least being that the peer group may have higher growth prospects in which case they would merit a higher-pea valuation.
[04:11 - 04:18] In fact, the underlying value drivers that listed here below is going to help us really understand them.
[04:18 - 04:23] If you parse out what a multiple implies, what drives them multiple.
[04:23 - 04:29] If you really ask, why should one company trade a 10 times PE while another should trade at 40 times PE?
[04:29 - 04:35] The reason is that there are really three variables that should fundamentally drive a multiple.
[04:35 - 04:42] The return on equity, in other words, the higher the company's return on equity, the higher the PE ratio should be.
[04:42 - 04:45] The next variable should be the growth rate.
[04:45 - 04:49] The higher the growth rate, the higher the PE ratio should be.
[04:49 - 04:53] And last but not least, the discount rate.
[04:53 - 04:56] Specifically for equity value multiples, that's the cost of equity.
[04:56 - 04:59] The higher the cost of equity, the lower the multiple should be.
[04:59 - 05:03] Those are the three things that should drive the PE ratio as a multiple, fundamentally.
[05:03 - 05:12] And so, when you're sort of thinking about what's driving the multiple, if you're comparing companies with different ROE prospects, with a different return on equity,
[05:12 - 05:17] with different growth, long-term growth rate expectations, with different costs of capital.
[05:17 - 05:22] Well, those are the things that are going to justifiably lead to differences across PE ratios.
[05:22 - 05:30] A comps analysis only works if you feel like in aggregate, the peers generally have the same fundamental prospects.
[05:30 - 05:32] Only then is it a useful comps analysis.
[05:32 - 05:38] So, if the peer group generally has the same ROE, the same long-term growth rate, the same cost of equity.
[05:38 - 05:44] Then you can say that, oh, my peer group has a higher PE ratio than mine.
[05:44 - 05:50] But since I have similar fundamental drivers, then I can say that maybe my company is undervalued or vice versa.
[05:50 - 05:53] Maybe the peer group is overvalued.
[05:53 - 06:01] But what makes comps particularly challenging, conceptually, is understanding that only if these are all generally the same across the peer group,
[06:01 - 06:06] does the PE ratio become useful for determining whether a company is overvalued or undervalued?
[06:06 - 06:13] The PE ratio is most appropriate for companies that are in a mature phase in their business cycle and their life cycle.
[06:13 - 06:19] And that's because generally those companies tend to have similar growth rates, similar costs of capital.
[06:19 - 06:28] Companies with positive earnings, obviously, a PE ratio for a company with negative earnings per share and negative net income, is going to lead to nonsensical results.
[06:28 - 06:30] Lastly, companies with similar capital structures.
[06:30 - 06:38] The reason why that's important for a PE ratio, and we talked about this in prior slide, is that the denominator is a levered metric.
[06:38 - 06:46] In fact, any time the denominator, in this case, earnings per share and net income, is after the impact of leverage, in other words,
[06:46 - 06:53] after the effect of interest expense or principal payments, you are implicitly accounting for that leverage in the multiple.
[06:53 - 07:01] And therefore, if you want a proper apples to apples comparison, you really should only be comparing against companies with similar leverage.
[07:01 - 07:03] Next, we turn to the PE ratio.
[07:03 - 07:06] The PE ratio is basically a PE ratio divided by the company's long-term growth rate.
[07:06 - 07:14] It solves one of the problems that we address in the prior slide, namely the assumption that the companies that are being compared have the same growth rate.
[07:14 - 07:24] So this is a multiple that you can use to compare companies with different long-term growth rates, but presumably similar returns on equity and cost of equity.
[07:24 - 07:30] Same issues exist in terms of EPS being a poor proxy for cash flows,
[07:30 - 07:33] we're only looking at EPS as of one period at a time.
[07:33 - 07:40] It still suffers from all the accounting issues that we talked about that include non-cash items and non-recurring items, etc.
[07:40 - 07:49] So you can do a lot of things to try to standardize for that, but off the shelf, a peg ratio, just like a PE ratio suffers from a lot of these accounting problems.
[07:49 - 07:53] It's also meaningless for companies where the earnings per share is negative.

---

### 70. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Convertible-Debt

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Convertible-Debt/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Convertible-Debt.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Convertible-Debt\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Convertible-Debt.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] Convertible debt works the same way as preferred stock from a make it from a dilution test standpoint
[00:05 - 00:11] The difference between convertible debt and convertible preferred stock is a convertible debt is convert is converted into common shares
[00:12 - 00:17] Where whereas the conversion price test uses the book value that convertible debt and
[00:18 - 00:24] Divides by the common shares debt is convertible into it's basically the same thing except instead of preferred stock you have debt
[00:25 - 00:27] You're employing the same test
[00:27 - 00:31] The main difference is when you're doing the dilute over anti dilute of test
[00:32 - 00:38] What you're what you're including in the net income to common on the basic EPS calculation is
[00:39 - 00:44] The interest that's paid on convertible debt, but when you're doing the diluted EPS calculation
[00:44 - 00:46] You're not excluding dividends
[00:46 - 00:50] You're not excluding preferred dividends as you had done with the dividend with a convertible preferred
[00:50 - 00:54] But what you're doing is excluding convertible interest expense

### 73. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Restricted-Stock

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Restricted-Stock/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Restricted-Stock.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Restricted-Stock\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Shares-Outstanding--Restricted-Stock.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:07] In addition to stock options, we need to address the treatment of restricted stock in the calculation of diluted shares outstanding.
[00:07 - 00:12] Now, since companies started having to expense stock options on the income statement several years ago,
[00:12 - 00:19] companies have begun to increasingly rely on simply issuing restricted stock instead of stock options as a form of stock based compensation.
[00:19 - 00:23] Now, the question is for us, how do we treat that and that diluted share count?
[00:23 - 00:29] And there are really two things that we have to think about. One is the treatment of restricted shares that have vested.
[00:29 - 00:34] That's pretty easy. Like options, restricted stock vest over several years.
[00:34 - 00:38] But when they vest, they automatically get included in the actual share count.
[00:38 - 00:42] So there's no dilute of impact. It'll simply be part of the basic share count.
[00:42 - 00:46] Now, the question of what do you do with unvested restricted shares?
[00:46 - 00:53] So similarly to unvested options, unvested restricted shares are typically not included in the diluted share count.
[00:53 - 00:55] We basically pretend like they don't exist.
[00:55 - 01:01] Now, that's obviously just from a logical standpoint, maybe not the ideal approach when you have a lot of restricted stock or options for that matter.
[01:01 - 01:04] In an alternative approach, that would be more conceptual.
[01:04 - 01:11] The rigorous might be to apply an illiquidity discount to unvested restricted shares and to include that in the diluted share count.
[01:11 - 01:17] Under the thinking, the employees that are holding these restricted shares haven't passed through their investing restrictions,
[01:17 - 01:21] but they most likely will. A lot of companies have two-year investing periods.
[01:21 - 01:24] So the vast majority of restricted shares end up vesting.
[01:24 - 01:29] And so we should certainly at least consider a portion of those unvested shares in the share count,
[01:29 - 01:34] allowing for some sort of illiquidity discount, allowing for the reality that some of these will be canceled.
[01:34 - 01:40] Now, completely ignoring unvested restricted stock, as most practitioners do, and we will actually do that.
[01:40 - 01:44] We can get away with it when the impacts are somewhat immaterial, but completely ignoring it.
[01:44 - 01:50] When it is material, we'll lead to a potential material underestimation of market cap and enterprise value.
[01:50 - 02:00] And just so we understand what these actual disclosures look like in real life, they're usually the disclosures for restricted stock are usually in the same place as the stock option disclosures,
[02:00 - 02:03] which is the stock based compensation footnote.
[02:03 - 02:10] And here we see F5 networks, which has some restricted stock outstanding. They usually vest quarterly over a two-year period.
[02:10 - 02:18] And so we have the balance of existing unvested restricted stock is 926,000.
[02:18 - 02:30] We are completely going to ignore those unrestricted or unvested restricted shares to be consistent with sort of what is the typical diluted share count calculation approach,
[02:30 - 02:32] but just understand the limitation of that.
[02:32 - 02:36] And the same limitation exists for basically ignoring unvested options.
[02:36 - 02:43] It's not going to be a big deal when the numbers aren't significant when the restricted stock is not a significant percentage of the total share count,
[02:43 - 02:49] but when it is a significant percentage, it may be something worth considering applying an illiquidity discount.

[00:00 - 00:03] Next, we turn to stock splits.

[00:08 - 00:11] to the split must be adjusted to reflect the split.
[00:11 - 00:15] Otherwise, the market share price will reflect a post-split price while the share count will
[00:15 - 00:20] be pre-split, leading to a huge underestimation of market count.
[00:20 - 00:24] To avoid this, always confirm that no split is taking place subsequent to the latest financial
[00:24 - 00:25] report.

[01:41 - 01:43] value of this company is not $5 billion.

[00:00 - 00:06] In this video, we address one of the more tricky issues that have to do with spreading
[00:06 - 00:07] comps.

[00:12 - 00:17] outstanding, accounting for convertible securities, dealing with things like stock splits,

[00:20 - 00:24] First of all, you should use diluted shares instead of the actual basic shares when you're
[00:24 - 00:26] calculating market cap.
[00:26 - 00:30] And that's because the market share price accounts for not just the actual shares outstanding,
[00:30 - 00:34] but also potentially dilutive securities, and ignoring that in the share count would
[00:34 - 00:35] understate the market cap of the business.

[00:53 - 00:55] going to have the price for each slice be $2.

[01:19 - 01:23] the price of each slice, you have to figure out exactly how many slices are in the pie.

[02:08 - 02:09] stock.

[02:33 - 02:36] The differences in terms of how they're issued, whereas stock options are usually given

[02:42 - 02:44] in a debt offering mechanically.

[02:51 - 02:55] And then when warrants are attached to debt, which is usually the case, the debt remains
[02:55 - 02:58] after the warrants are exercised.
[02:58 - 03:01] Convertible bonds are bonds that the company issues that can be converted into common stock

[03:03 - 03:06] So in this case, the convertible bond no longer is a bond.
[03:06 - 03:11] It becomes common stock unlike warrants, which when they're attached to debt, the debt remains
[03:11 - 03:13] after the warrants are exercised.
[03:13 - 03:14] So this is just an attachment.

[03:22 - 03:26] giving a more ownership control than necessary or end or entice investors except lower
[03:26 - 03:31] interest rates and they would normally accept on a straight debt issue.

[03:38 - 03:40] you can convert upon certain provisions.
[03:40 - 03:42] But up till now, we sort of taken forgiven.

[03:46 - 03:47] market capital.
[03:47 - 03:48] Why?
[03:48 - 03:49] Why is diluted important?
[03:49 - 03:53] So let's go through a couple of exercises that are going to make it hopefully crystal clear
[03:53 - 03:56] why you need diluted.
[03:56 - 04:03] So imagine a business with an equity value $500 million and has $100 million shares outstanding.
[04:03 - 04:07] What would you expect the company share price to be if that's all that existed in terms
[04:07 - 04:08] of capital base?
[04:08 - 04:13] Well at $500 million and $100 million shares outstanding, you're looking at $5 per share.
[04:13 - 04:16] The share count is simply $100 million shares outstanding.
[04:16 - 04:18] That's pretty straightforward.

[04:21 - 04:23] All right.

### 77. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:07] Price to book multiples are not for useful for most industries, but one particular industry commercial banks
[00:07 - 00:14] It's really useful for and it's widely used in that industry price to book defined as equity value divided by the book value of equity
[00:14 - 00:22] Has the same issue as price to earnings where it could actually be defined in two ways equity value divided by book value of equity or equity value
[00:22 - 00:28] For shared divided by book value for share. We know that the share count issue that goes with numerator and denominator here
[00:28 - 00:34] There's also one other way to think about book value price to book ratios, and that's tangible book value
[00:34 - 00:39] So book value is often adjusted to exclude goodwill and we call that tangible book value
[00:39 - 00:45] So if you take equity value which you can think of as assets minus liabilities if you exclude goodwill from the asset side
[00:45 - 00:54] What you have is tangible book value so for non banks for a imagine a consumer products company or retail business or a technology company
[00:54 - 00:58] The value of the assets and the value of liabilities measured at book value
[00:59 - 01:04] generally understate the true value of the business and that's because the assets and liabilities are
[01:04 - 01:06] recognized at a historical cost and
[01:07 - 01:17] You're not allowed to write up the value of the assets or liabilities under US gap or IFRS now with financial institutions whose primary assets and liabilities are financial instruments
[01:17 - 01:24] financial instruments with readily observable market value they are allowed to be written up or down to reflect market value
[01:24 - 01:26] And so financial institutions at hold
[01:27 - 01:33] Securities as their primary assets and have liabilities be readily have values that readily observable
[01:33 - 01:41] Well, their book values are constantly being written up or down to reflect true market values and therefore you can use book value as
[01:42 - 01:49] A good proxy for what the market value the business should be and then when you compare the market equity value to the book value
[01:49 - 01:52] What you're really comparing is how much the market?
[01:53 - 01:58] Values the entire enterprise or the entire equity in excess of
[01:58 - 02:02] The true market values of all the individual assets
[02:02 - 02:07] So it gives you a better perspective of what actually whether a business is potentially overvalued or undervalued
[02:07 - 02:11] Whereas if you use price to book for any other industry it would be a useless metric
[02:11 - 02:19] You couldn't really compare across different companies because they all may have different companies may have purchased assets at different times
[02:19 - 02:26] Comparability becomes useless price to book very useful for financial institutions and so for example
[02:26 - 02:33] What you could you can imagine saying that a company with a high price to book and usually financial institutions trade around one times price to book
[02:33 - 02:37] The higher they trade above one times price to book
[02:37 - 02:43] You can think of it. That is the company trading at a premium to book and you then have to explain why there is that premium
[02:43 - 02:49] What is it about the business that justifies a valuation above the book value of assets?

### 79. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-Overview

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-Overview/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-Overview.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-Overview\transcript\Trading-Comps-Modeling-Trading-Comps-Overview.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] We've already touched on this a little bit in the prior slide, so let's discuss this in a little bit more detail now.
[00:05 - 00:11] In addition to the need to standardize for size differences, other non-operational differences should be taken into account
[00:11 - 00:17] so as to not distort comparisons when you're value in companies by using other companies as comps.
[00:17 - 00:21] Probably the biggest thing to standardize in addition to size is leverage.
[00:21 - 00:25] And we saw this with the equity value multiples versus the enterprise value multiples.
[00:25 - 00:31] Specifically, the enterprise value numerator is a measure of value independent of leverage.
[00:31 - 00:39] Revenue, EBITDA, and EBIT as well as unlevered free cash flow denominators provide a measure of profit that's independent of leverage.
[00:39 - 00:41] It doesn't matter how much debt the company's taken on.
[00:41 - 00:46] The four metrics that we just addressed are not going to be any different depending on the leverage.
[00:46 - 00:50] There are before interest, there are before principal payments.
[00:50 - 00:57] In addition to financial leverage differences, we also may want to consider standardizing across different accounting differences.
[00:57 - 01:01] Things like depreciation method or useful life assumptions.
[01:01 - 01:08] So if we use profit metrics before depreciation and amortization like revenue or EBITDA or unlevered free cash flows,
[01:08 - 01:12] we are standardizing for accounting differences.
[01:12 - 01:15] In other words, we're ignoring accounting differences.
[01:15 - 01:21] Imagine two identical companies now keep in mind two identical companies should theoretically have identical multiples.
[01:21 - 01:23] But what if there was a difference?
[01:23 - 01:28] It's simply an accounting difference that has nothing to do with impact on cash or taxes.
[01:28 - 01:30] It's just a simple accounting difference.
[01:30 - 01:37] Specifically, one with a useful life assumption for all of its assets within average of 20 years.
[01:37 - 01:43] And the other company, for the exact same assets, chose to have a useful life assumption of 15 years.
[01:43 - 01:47] Well, if you look at EBIT for those companies, it's going to be different across the two companies.
[01:47 - 01:49] Whereas enterprise value is going to be the same.
[01:49 - 01:56] So you're going to have a higher multiple for the company with the higher depreciation expense or the lower useful life assumption.
[01:56 - 01:57] That shouldn't be the case.
[01:57 - 01:59] You want to look at two identical companies.
[01:59 - 02:01] They should have the same exact multiple presumably.
[02:01 - 02:08] Well, if you move up the income statement and you look at something like EBITDA, some of the major accounting differences
[02:08 - 02:14] differences that you see across companies like the depreciation method approach that you choose whether you strain line are accelerated
[02:14 - 02:16] or what useful life assumption you're going to make.
[02:16 - 02:23] You're going to be able to ignore that if you go up the income statement instead of you focusing on earnings
[02:23 - 02:27] or earnings before interest in taxes all the way up to EBITDA or revenue.
[02:27 - 02:31] Now, standardizing for this, so looking at EBITDA as opposed to EBIT,
[02:31 - 02:35] is going to be useful for companies with comparable levels of capital intensity.
[02:35 - 02:36] Otherwise, it's dangerous.
[02:36 - 02:45] Imagine two companies, one is extremely capital intensive and the other one isn't and you are basically ignoring the capital charge of depreciation expense
[02:45 - 02:50] or the accrual methods way to look at capital charges, depreciation expense.
[02:50 - 03:01] If you're going to ignore that, you are going to start getting skewed results because you're not recognizing the expense associated with a company being capital intensive.
[03:01 - 03:06] Next, we turn to temporary distortions, primarily we're talking about non-recurring items.
[03:06 - 03:10] When you're using multiples, they use historical profits as the denominator.
[03:10 - 03:20] Those profits have to be scrubbed to exclude distorted one-time items such as restructuring expenses, litigation costs and one-time gains on sale.
[03:20 - 03:27] Other accounting differences, like differences between how companies classify leases, whether one company classifies a leases,
[03:27 - 03:37] capital lease, and the other may classify that same leases and operating lease, that's going to have a material difference potentially when the companies have a lot of leases on what the financials look like.
[03:37 - 03:46] Similarly, life-overses, FIFO decisions for inventory is going to make a difference in terms of what cost of goods sold is identified on the income statement.
[03:46 - 03:54] Whether these issues rise to the level of having to be dealt with depends on the industry and the specific companies in question.
[03:54 - 04:08] Companies that have a lot of inventory and the difference between life-o and inventory is going to be significant, that's going to be something where it makes sense to standardize all companies to one inventory approach so that you can actually compare on an apples to apples basis.
[04:08 - 04:15] And the same goes for leases. Companies that don't have a lot of leases, you're not going to have to really worry about this lease classification adjustment.
[04:15 - 04:19] Companies with a lot of leases, that's something you need to start worrying about.
[04:19 - 04:28] Lastly, business life cycle differences. So comparable companies operationally may be at different phases of their life cycle, early stage versus growth versus maturity versus decline.
[04:28 - 04:35] This is somewhat of a art as opposed to science, determining where a company is in its business cycle.
[04:35 - 04:48] But companies that have high growth expectations, in other words, that they're at an early stage versus low growth, even if they're in the same line of business, you may want to consider standardizing against their long-term growth rate expectations.
[04:48 - 04:55] So multiples like the peg ratio really standardizes against different long-term growth rate expectations.
[04:55 - 05:02] While other multiples like EV to revenue or EV to EBITDA facilitate comparisons for early stage companies which are generating losses.
[05:02 - 05:16] So there are ways in which you can deal with not only companies at early stages that aren't generating profits, but even inter business life cycle comparisons are doable if you standardize against the different growth rate expectations.

---

### 82. Trading-Comps-Modeling-Unusual-or-Infrequent-Items

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Unusual-or-Infrequent-Items/transcript/Trading-Comps-Modeling-Unusual-or-Infrequent-Items.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:08] Now, let's talk about the cluster of income and expenses that FASB feels should not be excluded
[00:08 - 00:12] from the main income statement, and therefore requires companies to embed them within the
[00:12 - 00:18] income statement, but that analysts do frequently want to remove them from the income
[00:18 - 00:22] statement results so that they can hone in on core operating profitability.
[00:22 - 00:28] These types of items include restructuring expenses, one-time write-offs, gains in losses
[00:28 - 00:35] on sale of assets, severance costs, litigation gains in losses, these five clusters of income
[00:35 - 00:43] and expenses tend to be the kinds of things that FASB doesn't think are unusual and infrequent
[00:43 - 00:50] like extraordinary items or discontinued operations or accounting changes, but they are somewhat
[00:50 - 00:51] unusual.
[00:51 - 00:55] So FASB sort of identifies it if you're unusual or infrequent, but you're not both, just sort
[00:55 - 01:00] of an arbitrary sort of way to designate how non-recurring you are.
[01:00 - 01:05] And those items, FASB actually requires it, companies report pre-tax within the income
[01:05 - 01:06] statement.
[01:06 - 01:08] Analysts generally don't agree.
[01:08 - 01:12] They look at things like restructuring expenses, one-time write-offs, gains in losses in sale
[01:12 - 01:16] of assets, severance costs, and they say, we want to look at this company's operations
[01:16 - 01:22] without the noise of these things because we think that these are being unusual or infrequent,
[01:22 - 01:26] these are things that are going to skew our ability to compare this company's current
[01:26 - 01:29] results against prior period results.
[01:29 - 01:35] This company's current results against other companies' results, as well as gauge what
[01:35 - 01:41] they may do in the future, how these items may also impact our ability to forecast properly.
[01:41 - 01:46] So analysts like to exclude these from the results.
[01:46 - 01:51] Walmart not only has discontinued operations, but they also have these unusual or infrequent
[01:51 - 01:52] times.
[01:52 - 01:55] So let's take a look at what's going on in this slide.
[01:55 - 02:03] Walmart discloses in the footnotes that they had restructuring charges of $260 million.
[02:03 - 02:07] And they've also identified that they've embedded them with an operating, selling general
[02:07 - 02:10] administrative expenses.
[02:10 - 02:14] Restructuring charges are things that do not rise to the level in FASB's eyes of being
[02:14 - 02:20] truly non-recurring and therefore being allowed to be represented below the income statement,
[02:20 - 02:23] they have to be lumped into these core line items.

[02:55 - 02:56] item.

[02:59 - 03:04] a company or what FASB requires a company's present under gap.

[03:08 - 03:15] expense, if you're going to pretend as an analyst like that restructuring charge didn't
[03:15 - 03:21] happen, which is basically what ignoring it is, it also means you have to adjust taxes.

[03:27 - 03:32] pre-tax line items, that's the case here, the pre-tax restructuring charge.

[03:37 - 03:47] flat, then you're also going to have to pretend like the tax shield that having incremental
[03:47 - 03:50] expenses creates isn't going to be there.
[03:50 - 03:56] And that's what this slide is about that when presented, when restructuring items or non-recurring
[03:56 - 04:03] items are presented pre-tax, eliminating these non-recurring items should be accompanied
[04:03 - 04:04] by the elimination of their tax impact.
[04:04 - 04:08] And the way to think about it is that eliminating non-recurring items is like pretending
[04:08 - 04:09] that they didn't occur.
[04:09 - 04:14] And if you extend this logic, if a non-recurring expenses ignored, so should the associated
[04:14 - 04:15] tax shield.

[00:00 - 00:04] Before we start modeling, let's review some basic valuation concepts.
[00:04 - 00:09] So if I own a home, I'm concerned primarily with the equity value of the home,
[00:09 - 00:12] or am I concerned primarily with the total value of the home?
[00:12 - 00:13] So that's one question to ask.
[00:13 - 00:18] Another question to ask about valuation is, am I concerned with the original price that I paid,
[00:18 - 00:21] which we call the book value, or what it's currently worth,
[00:21 - 00:23] which is called the market value?
[00:23 - 00:26] Many of you might be thinking I'm primarily concerned with the market value,
[00:26 - 00:28] and that's usually true.

[00:49 - 00:53] And that's called this counter cash flow valuation or intrinsic valuation.

[02:11 - 02:16] you're going to be asked to designate a number of shares that represent the capital base for this company.

[02:21 - 02:25] the initial value of each share is just $5 per share.
[02:25 - 02:27] Pretty straightforward.

[02:31 - 02:35] So you obviously need, you can't just have the $950,000 just sitting there.
[02:35 - 02:40] You use some of the cash that was for a moment sitting in your bank account to purchase inventory and equipment.
[02:40 - 02:47] In addition to using cash to buy this stuff, $20,000 of the equipment that we purchased was not paid for with cash,
[02:47 - 02:48] instead it was invoiced.
[02:48 - 02:54] So we actually have $900,000 of inventory and property plan equipment,

[03:04 - 03:06] of equipment without paying for any cash.
[03:06 - 03:10] So now this is what your balance sheet looks like. Pretty straightforward.

[03:16 - 03:18] we can actually do that.
[03:18 - 03:26] Understand that the enterprise value, the definition that I suspect many of you are comfortable with is that enterprise value equals equity value plus net debt.
[03:26 - 03:28] That's absolutely true.
[03:28 - 03:35] But that's defining enterprise value as a function of what's on the right side of the balance sheet of how that enterprise value is funded.
[03:35 - 03:44] What I'd like to do here is make sure that we understand how enterprise, what enterprise value is from the perspective of what types of assets it represents.

[03:46 - 03:47] Let's be specific.
[03:47 - 03:52] Enterprise value equals the value of operating assets minus operating liabilities.
[03:52 - 04:02] Where specifically the value of operating assets, generally for most companies represent all assets except for cash as well as cash equivalents and long term investments.
[04:02 - 04:06] Cash is usually treated as a non operating asset and is netted against debt.
[04:06 - 04:13] Now that may differ with perhaps some academic treatment of cash where you buy for kick cash into excess cash versus,
[04:13 - 04:20] or you buy for kick cash into non operating asset called excess cash versus cash that's tied up in operations.
[04:20 - 04:26] That may conceptually make sense, but in practice they treat the entire cash balances and non operating asset.
[04:26 - 04:31] So treating cash as a non operating asset is basically akin to saying I can always use my cash to pay down debt.
[04:31 - 04:44] So rather than thinking about the amount of debt you have as a separate thing from the amount of cash you have, most practitioners just sort of aggregate the two together and think of a concept called net debt net of cash.
[04:44 - 04:47] Operating liabilities represent all liabilities except for debt.

[05:09 - 05:19] Okay, hopefully that was fairly straightforward enterprise value given the definition that we just provided where it represents the value of the operating assets minus the value of the operating liabilities.
[05:19 - 05:23] Well, we know that the value of the operating assets just going back here.
[05:23 - 05:27] We have inventory 500, property plan equivalent of 420.
[05:27 - 05:30] That's 920 but we had an operating liability of 20.
[05:30 - 05:32] That's 900,000.
[05:32 - 05:38] So that gets us the enterprise value and to get from enterprise value to equity value we subtract net debt.
[05:38 - 05:39] Well, what's net debt?
[05:39 - 05:44] We have a gross debt balance of 500,000 but we've got 50,000 in cash.
[05:44 - 05:55] So net debt is 450 and just like I suspect you're all comfortable with to get from enterprise value to equity value you just subtract that net debt and you get to 450.
[05:55 - 06:07] The reason why we defined enterprise value in this way that might be a little less familiar to folks is to get you thinking about the idea that enterprise value represents the value of the operating assets net of the liabilities.
[06:07 - 06:12] Don't just think about it in the context of how it's financed but think about it in the context of what it actually is.
[06:12 - 06:24] Also recognize that enterprise value minus net debt equals equity value that that ubiquitous equation that you see in used in investment banking and corporate finance.
[06:24 - 06:32] Understand that that is just a simple reformulation of the basic accounting equation assets equal liabilities plus equity.
[06:32 - 06:35] It's simply moving certain things around.
[06:35 - 06:51] If you subtract from both sides of here cash so assets excluding cash and then you subtract cash from liabilities and then you move certain operating liabilities from this side you subtract it from this side but also subtract it from this side there you have it.
[06:51 - 07:02] You've arrived at enterprise value equals net debt plus equity value and then if you move the net debt from one side to the other you've just you're moving terms around.
[07:02 - 07:08] Why is it important it's because it's a common way to discuss value so make sure you're comfortable the basic intuition.
[07:08 - 07:18] So far we looked at value from the perspective of the original investment in the business or book value and of course most businesses are worth far more than the book value and their balance sheet would suggest.
[07:18 - 07:27] Presumably as you start operating your hot dog stand and you start generating profits and word on the street is that you've got the best hot dogs in town.
[07:27 - 07:42] Well the value of your business the reputation of your business starts enabling you to generate cash flows that lead to a valuation for your company that is far more than simply the value of your inventories and your property plan equipment.
[07:42 - 07:58] That's when in fact you observe in the market with company with public companies and that's in fact the case with private companies as well that the value of their business is not simply worth the the summation of all the book values that represent the historical values of the assets that they purchased in the past.
[07:58 - 08:06] So what tools do we have to determine the actual market value of a business well for publicly traded companies the equity market value is readily observable.
[08:06 - 08:12] That's simply the share price times the shares outstanding we call that the market cap or the market capitalization.
[08:12 - 08:18] And if you look at Google for example and compare its book value to its market value you can see a massive difference.
[08:18 - 08:22] Google has an equity book value of 75 billion dollars.
[08:22 - 08:31] Now that's the book value that represents the value of equity if you consider only the book value of the assets and the book value of the liabilities.
[08:31 - 08:35] If you look at the market Google's shares are trading at eight hundred and seventy five dollars a share.
[08:35 - 08:43] And they have three hundred and thirty seven million shares outstanding which implies a market value or a market cap of two hundred ninety billion dollars.
[08:43 - 08:50] Compare to ninety billion to seventy five billion the market cap here is much bigger than the book value would suggest.
[08:50 - 09:16] Google has fifty billion dollars in cash five billion in debt per the company's latest queue implying a enterprise value of two ninety minus forty five billion that the enterprise value the value of its operating assets let its liabilities is two hundred forty five billion here we clearly see a disconnect between the market values and the book value.
[09:16 - 09:25] Before we talk about trading comp specifically as a valuation method let's sort of put an umbrella around it how does it fit within the overall valuation umbrella.
[09:25 - 09:32] First we have to address the two pillars of valuation relative valuation versus DCF valuation.
[09:32 - 09:41] So while intrinsic valuation or DCF valuation is derived from the fundamental analysis of a company's cash flow generation potential.
[09:41 - 09:51] Relative valuation or comps is derived by simply comparing the company to its comparable peers and essentially looking at the value of its peer group.
[09:51 - 09:58] And saying well if the peer group is valued at X amount that is a valuation that makes sense for this company.
[09:58 - 10:07] So why we use trading comps to value companies we do this to determine what the appropriate value of the company is based on the market values of operationally similar companies.
[10:07 - 10:13] If you think about looking at the value of a house when you're actually trying to figure out what is the value of your house.
[10:13 - 10:18] You do that by looking at the the values of similar houses in the neighborhood that's a comps analysis.
[10:18 - 10:28] Now the challenge with companies is opposed to houses is that they're trickier to value because you can't ever really find a truly comparable company.
[10:28 - 10:30] No two companies are truly alike.
[10:30 - 10:40] Whereas houses you look at two houses in the neighborhood have the same square footage built in the same way, have access to the same school system, the same tenant base.
[10:40 - 10:42] Those are virtually identical types of assets.
[10:42 - 10:44] Whereas with companies it's a little more difficult.
[10:44 - 10:51] So what you end up doing is standardizing instead of looking at absolute values and just comparing absolute values the way you look at with houses.
[10:51 - 10:54] You standardize across different factors.
[10:54 - 10:59] So you may have two companies that are very similar except that one is much bigger than the other.
[10:59 - 11:02] You don't compare the absolute values but what you do is you look at multiples.
[11:02 - 11:13] You standardize against various measures of a firm's profitability and some of the most common ways to standardize or to compare is to use multiples like enterprise value to eBITDA multiple.
[11:13 - 11:24] Where you're comparing, you're not comparing absolute enterprise values but you're benchmarking them against the peer groups, the individual companies eBITDA ratios.
[11:24 - 11:31] So what you're comparing are multiples of eBITDA or multiples of revenue or multiples of eBIT.
[11:31 - 11:37] These three types are called enterprise value multiples because the numerator is enterprise value.
[11:37 - 11:41] Whereas on the other side you have equity value multiples.
[11:41 - 11:47] PE ratios, the company's share price divided by its earnings per share or the market cap divided by net income.
[11:47 - 11:49] These two are virtually identical.
[11:49 - 11:56] There are slight granular differences between using share price divided by EPS versus market cap by net income.
[11:56 - 11:59] And we'll talk about those later in the comps analysis.
[11:59 - 12:00] And then you have PE growth.
[12:00 - 12:02] That's another equity value multiple.
[12:02 - 12:10] And really the big picture differences between these two approaches is that enterprise value multiples really don't take into account any leverage differences across the company.
[12:10 - 12:12] The companies that are being compared.
[12:12 - 12:15] Imagine you're comparing your house against other houses.
[12:15 - 12:20] You're not really considering what the mortgages on any of these houses.
[12:20 - 12:28] The leverage issue isn't usually analyzed when you're thinking about buying a house because you're going to whatever asset you're going to buy.
[12:28 - 12:30] You're going to leverage your own rate.
[12:30 - 12:34] And so the idea here is that these types of multiples really don't consider leverage.
[12:34 - 12:39] Whereas on the equity multiple side, you're taking leverage into consideration.
[12:39 - 12:50] You're saying what is a PE multiple, for example, measures not only the company's operational performance in comparison across the peer group, but really also factors in things like leverage.
[12:50 - 13:00] And so a highly levered company that might be operationally identical to an unlevered company are going to have very different PE ratios.
[13:00 - 13:05] Whereas if you did that example on an enterprise value, you bet that basis.
[13:05 - 13:11] An unlevered company that's operationally identical to a levered company is going to have the exact same enterprise value you bet that mold.

### 87. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-1

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] A transaction comps analysis is only useful if you find truly comparable companies,
[00:04 - 00:10] and it's the old adage garbage in garbage out, doesn't matter how sleek your model is going to be.
[00:10 - 00:14] So what makes transaction comps more challenging than training comps, as I've already mentioned,
[00:14 - 00:20] is really the sparseness of information that's required to first select the comps to actually find
[00:20 - 00:25] the right transactions, and then to gather all the relevant documents for those transactions.
[00:25 - 00:30] Inputting and presenting results is very much like what you would do in a training comps analysis,
[00:30 - 00:33] so that's kind of the easy part, hard part is getting the data.
[00:33 - 00:40] And broadly speaking, there are three main places you go to find recent in relevant transactions,
[00:40 - 00:45] first is by talking to colleagues, seeing if there are existing comp sets that someone has already
[00:45 - 00:48] built for this industry, for the specific company that you're looking for.
[00:49 - 00:53] The second way you're going to look for transactions is probably the most common thing you're going to do,
[00:53 - 01:01] which is use a database like facts that are Bloomberg, etc. to screen for a variety of transactions
[01:01 - 01:05] in the particular industry that your target company is operating in.
[01:06 - 01:12] Third is looking for fairness opinions from comparable companies, and this is sort of a nice
[01:12 - 01:16] insider's tip for how to find transactions when approach number one and approach number two
[01:16 - 01:20] isn't working, where you're not getting a good transaction screen, and colleagues aren't really
[01:20 - 01:26] giving you much. You can look for comparable companies, look for whether they've done deals
[01:26 - 01:31] in the past, presumably a comparable company, that will do a deal, will do a similar type of
[01:31 - 01:37] will acquire similar types of businesses, and to the extent that they have filed fairness opinions
[01:37 - 01:42] within those deal filings, those fairness opinions in and of themselves will have aggregated
[01:42 - 01:47] potential transaction comp sets already. So we're going to talk about all three of those,
[01:47 - 01:52] we're going to start with the first. The best places to go are recommendations from colleagues,
[01:52 - 01:57] so that might shed light on which deals are most relevant. There may already be a
[01:57 - 02:01] comp set from a prior picture and deal that can serve as a starting point. So before you do all
[02:01 - 02:05] the heavy lifting of really starting from scratch, just go through the simple steps of just asking
[02:05 - 02:11] around, of course you want to be reasonable about this if the expectation is that you do this
[02:11 - 02:14] from scratch, you got to do it from scratch, but to the extent that there are folks out there that
[02:14 - 02:20] can help you that understand the industry, that's a good place to start. So the more challenging
[02:20 - 02:23] is when you don't have someone telling you exactly what the good deals are and that's when you're on
[02:23 - 02:29] your own. So by far the most common thing that people use and the most useful resource is sort of
[02:29 - 02:37] accessing a data base that has accumulated comparable transaction. So capital IQ, SNL, which is
[02:37 - 02:41] industry specific for banks, et cetera, facts at Bloomberg and Thompson, those are more broad. So
[02:41 - 02:46] capital IQ facts at Bloomberg and Thompson tend to be the broader databases. They really compile past
[02:46 - 02:52] deal data, a lot of past deal data and allow you to find deals using various screening parameters.
[02:52 - 02:57] And on the right here we've identified what are some of the most common screening parameters.
[02:57 - 03:02] Probably the most common by far are the industry screens. So you can actually use various
[03:02 - 03:09] industry screen techniques like industry codes to look for companies, target companies typically
[03:09 - 03:13] that are in the same industry as the company that you're trying to analyze and that'll spit out
[03:13 - 03:18] deals where those similar companies from similar industries were in transactions. The way that you
[03:18 - 03:24] enhance that so you don't get hundreds of results from the same industry but may not be that relevant
[03:24 - 03:29] is you sort of streamline it for size. So you want a similar size to the company that you're looking
[03:29 - 03:34] at and by size you can look at enterprise value revenue, eBITDA to kind of make sure that you've
[03:35 - 03:40] that you're looking at something truly comparable. And then there are secondary or I should say before
[03:40 - 03:45] we get secondary how recent was the deal. So you may have had a deal from 15 years ago that
[03:45 - 03:51] was really comparable but it's too stale. And generally there's no absolute rule about what
[03:51 - 03:58] constitutes stale but you try not to go back more than six or seven years. Secondary factors or
[03:58 - 04:02] things like industry position, seasonality, cyclicality, how much leverage a company's got,
[04:02 - 04:07] different growth characteristics. But usually you end up getting sort of you focusing on the
[04:07 - 04:11] primary factors. It's only if you have a ton of transactions that choose from the truly comparable
[04:11 - 04:16] can you be picky and start looking at secondary factors. Always confirm the output
[04:16 - 04:22] data via SEC filings and news runs if you're using these database screens. One of the challenges
[04:22 - 04:27] with using Cap IQ or facts that are Bloomberg or Thompson or SNL is that they're really really good
[04:27 - 04:35] for screening deals but not necessarily for getting all the deal terms and financials correctly
[04:35 - 04:40] in their databases. There are often mistakes in these. And so if you want to sort of
[04:40 - 04:45] take advantage of what's available but not incorporate mistakes into your models, use these services
[04:45 - 04:50] to find the deals and then do the digging yourself for the actual deal terms. Ultimately what
[04:50 - 04:56] capital IQ does when they find the deals, they go through the SEC filings and try to fair it out
[04:56 - 04:59] the right data. And so you want to be the one going through that process to make sure the
[04:59 - 05:06] Cap IQ didn't make a mistake that's now going to get baked into your model.

### 88. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-2

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-2/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-2.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-2\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Finding-Comparable-Transactions-Part-2.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:14] So at this point, why don't we actually just get started and do a transaction screen in fact set so our case study is going to be extreme networks and we're going to want to try to find some comparable transactions where the target is comparable to extreme.
[00:14 - 00:20] So we're looking at extreme networks and we're trying to find transactions that are comparable where the target is comparable to extreme networks.

[00:00 - 00:04] Before we dive into the actual case study, there's one other issue that has to do with
[00:05 - 00:09] calculating shares outstanding, specifically diluted shares outstanding and transaction comps.
[00:09 - 00:14] It's actually even trickier than calculating diluted shares outstanding in a standard trading comps analysis.
[00:14 - 00:19] And the issue in terms of why this is important stems from the fact that terms,
[00:19 - 00:26] deal terms, are disclosed as usually either a total absolute purchase price, like $180 million in the
[00:26 - 00:32] terraces case. Or on a per share basis, we are buying this company for $25 per share in cash.
[00:32 - 00:39] Well, when the per share terms are explicit, correctly calculating the share count becomes extremely important
[00:39 - 00:45] because we need it to arrive at the total transaction value. If a company discloses, hey, that we're
[00:45 - 00:52] offering $25 per share in cash, that's the explicit offer term. Well, we then have to really search
[00:52 - 00:57] for what is the right share count? Because what we're trying to find is the transaction value. That's the
[00:57 - 01:03] numerator when calculating multiples, like EBITDA, et cetera. And the offer value is the numerator when
[01:03 - 01:09] we're looking at multiples, like offer value to eat per share divided by UPS. So we really have to figure out
[01:09 - 01:17] what that diluted share count looks like. Let's take a look at this disclosure issue and just kind of

[01:31 - 01:36] explicitly. That was the explicit offer terms. And then they tell you that that means that the deal is
[01:36 - 01:43] approximately $2.7 billion. How did they get from $7.98, $2.7? Well, presumably they've identified how
[01:43 - 01:48] many shares they think they're going to have to buy at $7.90. That process is the process of calculating
[01:48 - 01:53] diluted shares you have to take into account restricted stock options, potential convertibles.

[01:58 - 02:03] networks acquiring and terraces, when they say, hey, we're just going to buy the whole thing for $180
