[02:08 - 02:12] they don't even disclose the shares of standing. The issue becomes a little bit less important.
[02:12 - 02:17] But when deals are expressed in per share becomes very important to calculate correctly the share
[02:18 - 02:23] So in a trading comps analysis or when you're valuing a company like we did with Apple on a standalone
[02:23 - 02:28] DCF, you're going to find the latest basic share count by looking at the front cover of the latest
[02:28 - 02:33] 10K or 10Q. It's actually pretty similar in a transaction comps analysis. You're going to look at
[02:33 - 02:38] the front cover of the latest pre deal, 10Q or 10K. And remember, we talked about if the acquire
[02:38 - 02:43] management maybe had access to even a 10Q or 10K that made these few days post deal,
[02:43 - 02:47] or in the same day of the deal, then you want to use that. There's one other wrinkle with
[02:47 - 02:53] transaction comps. And that is that the merger agreement usually has more timely share counts. And the
[02:53 - 03:00] merger agreement is usually filed within the proxy. And so you can see that either filed separately
[03:00 - 03:06] as an 8K or in a in the proxy. So the merger agreement we usually has will usually have a share
[03:06 - 03:10] count that's the day over, day prior to the acquisition. And it's usually in a section titled
[03:10 - 03:14] capitalization. So this is maybe a lesser known place to find the latest share count, but it's
[03:14 - 03:19] actually usually even better than the latest pre deal, 10Q or 10K. One other thing to note about
[03:19 - 03:26] when target is not public is that you may find situations where you actually don't even have
[03:26 - 03:32] a pre deal, 10Q or 10K if the target is private. But you do have a merger agreement because perhaps
[03:32 - 03:37] the acquire is public. Now let's talk about delude of securities. How do you go about finding
[03:37 - 03:42] those? So in a training comps analysis, just as a refresher, you go to the latest 10Q or 10K
[03:42 - 03:47] to find pre deal dilute securities. We talked about the issue of how options when you have
[03:47 - 03:52] options outstanding. There's a there's a lag and so sometimes you have to go back to the 10K.
[03:52 - 03:57] Even if there's, you know, even if there's a more recent 10Q because there just aren't that
[03:57 - 04:04] many disclosures in the 10Q, it's a similar issue with transaction comps, except now you also want to
[04:04 - 04:10] go to the merger agreement. You may find the latest securities in the merger agreement in that
[04:10 - 04:15] same capitalization section. And just as a tip that we've mentioned throughout, if you're searching
[04:15 - 04:21] for dilute of securities disclosures, when you're trying to find options in all these documents,
[04:21 - 04:25] search for the term exerciseable, when you're looking for convertible search for the term
[04:25 - 04:30] convertible, with restricted stock look for RSU's or just any of these terms in that'll help
[04:30 - 04:35] you so you're not waiting through a 200 page document forever. In terms of options, let's
[04:35 - 04:38] spend a little bit more time on that because those dilute securities are the trickiest. So,
[04:38 - 04:44] training comps analysis, as you know, you want to use in the money extra sizable options and warrants,
[04:44 - 04:49] warrants are treated effectively the same way as options using the treasury stock method to
[04:49 - 04:54] calculate dilution. We've talked about that quite a bit. And the transaction comps analysis,
[04:54 - 04:59] it's the same mechanics. The difference though is we are going to use in the money
[04:59 - 05:05] outstanding options, not extra sizable options. And actually to be more specific, it's that
[05:05 - 05:12] standing or if you have it, this is actually better vested and expected to vest. As we'll talk
[05:12 - 05:17] about when you get into our case studies, outstanding options are all the options vested and expected
[05:17 - 05:22] to vest are all the options that are outstanding that are expected to vest and won't,
[05:22 - 05:28] for example, be expected to be forfeited or will not vest because whatever restrictions they
[05:28 - 05:34] have are not going to, you know, they're not going to meet those restrictions. So,
[05:35 - 05:40] so there's a difference there. And we're using the same treasury stock method. And the reason why
[05:40 - 05:44] we're using outstanding or vested and expected to vest is supposed to exerciseable is that
[05:44 - 05:48] in an acquisition, options often vest upon a change of control. And as we'll see shortly,
[05:48 - 05:55] even if the options don't vest upon a change of control, which is possible, you still want
[05:55 - 06:01] to use outstanding or vest and expected to vest because it presents a more realistic assessment
[06:01 - 06:06] if those options are expected to vest down the road of what an acquire really has to
[06:06 - 06:10] think about in terms of the overall value of the deal. They're ultimately going to have to issue
[06:10 - 06:16] consideration for options, even if they have invested quite yet. Now, let's talk about restricted
[06:16 - 06:21] stock. In a training comps analysis, we tend to exclude invested restricted stock from the
[06:21 - 06:27] diluted share count. We talked about how that may or may not be correct. The notion that excluding
[06:27 - 06:32] invested restricted stock from the diluted share count may really be underestimating the true market
[06:32 - 06:38] cap or the value of the business because it excludes these restricted shares that may ultimately become
[06:38 - 06:43] they will ultimately vest. And so in a transaction comps analysis, you actually do tend to take
[06:43 - 06:49] those into account. You include vested and expected to vest restricted stock as restricted stock not
[06:49 - 06:53] only often automatically vest upon a change of control, but again, just like with options,
[06:53 - 06:58] the logic is that when a company is acquiring a target that has significant amounts of restricted
[06:58 - 07:02] stock and those are just, you know, a year away from vesting or two years away from vesting,
[07:02 - 07:06] you should consider that with factoring. What is the actual, you know, what is the actual purchase
[07:06 - 07:12] price? Understanding that there is not an automatic vest in all deals, but even in the absence
[07:12 - 07:17] of an automatic vest, most practitioners do include the unvested in the money options and restricted
[07:18 - 07:21] stock. And the logic for including this was we've already talked about that they're expected to
[07:21 - 07:26] vest at some point and should thus be factored in as a real claim on the equity. Now that for
[07:26 - 07:30] those of you who are who just gone through our training comps course might be wondering, hey, this is
[07:30 - 07:35] isn't this a little bit of a contradiction from how we did things with our training comps course.
[07:35 - 07:39] It's a some extent you're right. A similar argument can be made for including unvested options
[07:39 - 07:43] and restricted stock when calculating diluted shares and training comps or even a standalone DCF.
[07:44 - 07:49] For better for worse, practitioner is rarely include unvested options in the diluted share count
[07:49 - 07:54] in those cases. I would actually argue that just conceptually makes sense to include the restricted
[07:54 - 07:59] shares that are invested in a standalone valuation as well. But that's just not frequently done.
[07:59 - 08:03] And the process of this course is to just walk you through what the best practices are. It
[08:03 - 08:07] doesn't mean that you shouldn't be mindful of some perhaps some contradictions and some issues
[08:07 - 08:12] that exist. That's what this is. But very rarely do you see in a standalone DCF or a
[08:12 - 08:18] training comps analysis that the restricted shares or the options that are outstanding but not
[08:18 - 08:28] exerciseable are counted. Whereas in the transaction comps they're always counted.

[00:00 - 00:01] The last thing that I want to address here
[00:01 - 00:06] is a timing mismatch that comes from options disclosures.
[00:06 - 00:09] So some companies only provide detailed options data
[00:09 - 00:12] in the 10K, but not in the 10K or merger agreement.
[00:12 - 00:15] And what that means is the latest basic share count, which
[00:15 - 00:18] is found in the latest 10K or the merger agreement,
[00:18 - 00:20] is more recent than the latest options data, which
[00:20 - 00:23] comes from the more stale 10K.
[00:23 - 00:25] The timing mismatch means that between the date
[00:25 - 00:28] of the options disclosures and the latest share count,
[00:28 - 00:31] options could have been exercised in or new options issued.
[00:31 - 00:33] And so you have some stale data,
[00:33 - 00:35] and you have this mismatch because you're
[00:35 - 00:38] using both recent and stale data.
[00:38 - 00:42] In practice, the mismatch is almost always ignored,
[00:42 - 00:43] unless there's reason to believe
[00:43 - 00:45] that significant changes to the number of options
[00:45 - 00:48] have occurred between the 10K disclosure date
[00:48 - 00:50] when you've got this really good disclosure
[00:50 - 00:53] for options date, data, and the either merger agreement
[00:53 - 00:56] or the latest 10Q date.

[01:00 - 01:03] would have been made public to the acquire.

[01:20 - 01:26] So you have to kind of be very careful about which latest 10K or 10Q you use.

[02:51 - 02:53] You have to do a lot of digging.

[00:00 - 00:09] So there are other potentially dilutive securities, like convertible debt, convertible preferred stock, restricted stock units, as well as other dilutive securities.
[00:09 - 00:15] So the way that we look for that is we go back to the 10Q, and we look for potentially dilutive securities.
[00:15 - 00:21] An easy way to go to the Q or K, type in the word convertible to see, are there any convertible securities?
[00:21 - 00:24] We went back to the K as well, there are no convertible securities.
[00:24 - 00:26] What about warrants?
[00:26 - 00:28] warrants are like options.
[00:28 - 00:30] This company has no warrants.
[00:30 - 00:33] They're typically associated with a convertible debt that a company has.
[00:33 - 00:36] So if a company has convertible debt, potentially they have some warrants.
[00:36 - 00:39] But most companies do have some restricted stock awards.
[00:39 - 00:41] Foundry's no exception.
[00:41 - 00:45] Let's look for restricted stock.
[00:45 - 00:47] And let's see if we can find this closure.
[00:47 - 00:50] Usually it's right around where the option data is.
[00:50 - 00:53] And here we are.
[00:53 - 00:57] So this is the stock option disclosure that we just looked at.
[00:57 - 00:59] And now let's look at the restricted stock disclosures.
[00:59 - 01:02] So they have two types of restricted stock.
[01:02 - 01:06] While there are some legal distinctions between the two that could be important from a tax standpoint,
[01:06 - 01:10] et cetera, from a dilution calculation standpoint.
[01:10 - 01:16] Whether you have restricted stock awards or restricted stock units, both of them affect dilution in the same way,
[01:16 - 01:18] in terms of how we calculate dilution is outstanding.
[01:18 - 01:20] So let's start with the first one.
[01:20 - 01:26] Restricted stock awards, there's a balance of 638,000 restricted stock awards.
[01:26 - 01:30] These, when they vest, become actual stock.
[01:30 - 01:34] And in an acquisition, several things can actually potentially happen.
[01:34 - 01:39] The first is just like with options where there's an automatic vesting of all restricted stock.
[01:39 - 01:44] And they immediately get the same form of consideration as any other common shareholder.
[01:44 - 01:54] The alternative is restricted stock stays invested whereby you're either promised that you're going to get that cash after the vesting period.
[01:54 - 02:00] Or that restricted stock converts into the stock of the acquire and maintains the same vesting restrictions.
[02:00 - 02:02] So let's see what happened in this deal.
[02:02 - 02:06] Foundry included a proxy for the deal.
[02:06 - 02:14] And usually in the proxy, there's an explicit explanation of what happens to restricted stock as well as options.
[02:14 - 02:17] What will happen to my restricted stock on page 13.
[02:17 - 02:21] Each share of our restricted common stock that is outstanding at the effect of time of the merger.
[02:21 - 02:30] And is invested or subject to risk of forfeiture will be exchanged into the right to receive $16.50 in cash without interest.
[02:30 - 02:46] Notice that it goes on to say, though, that however, unless otherwise provided under the stock purchase agreement or any other agreement, the cash will be received in exchange for such shares of restricted stock, but will remain invested and continue to be subject to the same risk of forfeiture and other conditions.
[02:46 - 02:52] It'll be held by brocade, the cash will be until those vesting restrictions lapse.
[02:52 - 02:59] And so this actually opens up a bit of a question of do we include these restricted shares in the diluted share count?
[02:59 - 03:07] Because they're still subject to vesting restrictions, there could be an argument made to keep them out of the diluted share count because they are invested.
[03:07 - 03:21] However, the prevailing best practice when spreading transaction comps and in contrast to the treatment of restricted stock and trading comps is to include any restricted stock that is expected to vest in the diluted share count.
[03:21 - 03:27] So we're going to include the 638,000 in the diluted share count. They're not in the basic share count.
[03:27 - 03:31] It's a very similar situation with restricted stock units.
[03:31 - 03:43] There's 1.379 million expected to vest and that makes sense because once restricted stock units vest, they're no longer restricted stock, they become common stock.
[03:43 - 03:49] And so the question is do you use the 1.6 million balance or the 1.4 million balance?
[03:49 - 03:56] The difference here between the total outstanding RSUs and the ones that are just expected to vest.
[03:56 - 04:00] Presumably are the ones that for whatever reason the company expects will not vest.
[04:00 - 04:03] And that's because they're either forfeited or they're expired.
[04:03 - 04:10] Let's go with, although it's not 100% clear, the general best practices to go with the ones that are expected to vest.
[04:10 - 04:14] So we're going to take this 1.379 plus the 638,000.
[04:14 - 04:19] Let's push that into our share count so we've already populated in.
[04:19 - 04:24] We also insert in a comment that we're using both the RSUs and their restricted stock wards and the pages closures where they are.
[04:24 - 04:27] At this point, I'd like you guys to incorporate this yourself.
[04:27 - 04:35] Put in all the data entry that we've looked at from diluted shares all the way to the very end of the calculation of the fully diluted shares.
[04:35 - 04:39] Make sure you understand it and found where the disclosures are at when you're ready to move to the next video.
[04:39 - 04:43] And then we're going to calculate net debt and ultimately transaction value.
[04:43 - 04:45] We have offer value now.
[04:45 - 04:51] We have everything in place which is 147 million fully diluted shares times the offer price.
[04:51 - 04:57] You have an offer value of 2.4 million that's consistent with what we saw in fact set where they said it was a 2.4 billion dollar deal.
[04:57 - 05:03] Now let's figure out what the actual transaction value is in order to do that we need to add net debt.

### 107. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Introduction-to-Transaction-Comps

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Introduction-to-Transaction-Comps/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Introduction-to-Transaction-Comps.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:02] Okay, let's get started.
[00:02 - 00:06] In this picture you see several almost identical houses.
[00:06 - 00:11] If you were thinking about buying this particular house, the one right in the middle, how would
[00:11 - 00:12] you value that house?
[00:12 - 00:17] Well, certainly if the house next door had recently been purchased and that purchase
[00:17 - 00:20] price was public, that would certainly provide a lot of information for you about what the
[00:20 - 00:22] right price to expect might be.
[00:22 - 00:26] In fact, when you try to gauge the fair value of your house by comparing to the values
[00:26 - 00:31] of houses nearby, you're doing a transaction comps analysis.
[00:31 - 00:35] Perhaps one of the main differences is that when you're doing a transaction comps analysis,
[00:35 - 00:37] you're comparing companies.
[00:37 - 00:41] And comparing companies is harder than houses because truly comparable companies are
[00:41 - 00:44] pretty hard to find.
[00:44 - 00:49] Even if you find comparable transactions where the target company is truly comparable to the
[00:49 - 00:53] company that you're trying to analyze, you have to standardize for differences like size.
[00:53 - 00:58] And so we use multiples instead of absolute values like we would with the house example
[00:58 - 01:00] in the prior slide.
[01:00 - 01:06] The most commonly used transaction multiples are TV over the target's EBITDA, where transaction
[01:06 - 01:09] value is another term for enterprise value.
[01:09 - 01:13] It's just the way that we refer to enterprise value in the context of M&A.
[01:13 - 01:18] TV to target revenue, TV to target EBITDA, these all represent what are the sort of typical
[01:18 - 01:23] enterprise value multiples and they're very much like the types of multiples you'd see in
[01:23 - 01:28] the trading comps analysis for those of you who've just completed our trading comps course.
[01:28 - 01:30] In addition, there are equity value multiples.
[01:30 - 01:34] Now equity value in the M&A context has a slightly different name.
[01:34 - 01:39] Offer price per share is really the equity value per share over the target EPS.
[01:39 - 01:42] That's equivalent of a PE ratio.
[01:42 - 01:44] Offer value over target net income.
[01:44 - 01:48] That's the PE ratio where you're looking at equity value over net income instead of equity
[01:48 - 01:52] value per share over earnings per share.
[01:52 - 01:56] Polls are derived from recent considerations provided by a choir to purchase similar
[01:56 - 01:57] companies in the past.
[01:57 - 02:03] Now for those of you who have not gone through the trading comps course yet, the remaining
[02:03 - 02:08] videos in this section are actually a review of certain portions of the trading comps
[02:08 - 02:10] course that I think are pretty critical for you.
[02:10 - 02:14] If you've already gone through the trading comps course, just skip on to the next section
[02:14 - 02:15] and start in section two.
[02:15 - 02:18] For those that haven't gone through the trading comps course, it's going to be very important
[02:18 - 02:20] that you go through the remaining videos in this section one.

### 112. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-PE--PEG-Ratios

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-PE--PEG-Ratios/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-PE--PEG-Ratios.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-PE--PEG-Ratios\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-PE--PEG-Ratios.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:07] In the next several slides, we are going to go through and define and highlight some issues with the most common multiples.
[00:07 - 00:13] Now, when you spread carbs, when you perform a comps analysis or when you perform any sort of valuation,
[00:13 - 00:17] what you'll recognize is that there are some ubiquitous multiples out there.
[00:17 - 00:21] EV to EBITDA is a multiple that's used across a wide array of industries,
[00:21 - 00:24] and the same goes for the PE ratio that we're going to introduce here.
[00:24 - 00:27] Other multiples are fairly industry-specific.
[00:27 - 00:33] Determining the multiple that's appropriate to use in your analysis is going to be a function of the industry that the company is in,
[00:33 - 00:35] and the specific state of the company.
[00:35 - 00:42] So, these are all decisions that you become more and more comfortable with and more you understand the business and the more you understand the industry.
[00:42 - 00:47] And what we're striving to do over the next several slides is just give you a sense of which industries are appropriate for which multiples.
[00:47 - 00:52] So, the PE ratio, probably the most common next to the EV to EBITDA multiple,
[00:52 - 00:54] probably the two most common multiples out there.
[00:54 - 01:00] There are two ways to actually define the PE ratio. One is to define it as price per share divided by earnings per share.
[01:00 - 01:04] The other way to define it is equity value divided by net income.
[01:04 - 01:11] Both ratios are actually considered interchangeable typically as price per share is really just equity value per share,
[01:11 - 01:15] whereas EPS is really just net income per share.
[01:15 - 01:21] Now, the one caveat to that is when you're trying to get to equity value from share price,
[01:21 - 01:24] you want to multiply by the latest shares outstanding,
[01:24 - 01:28] whereas to get from earnings per share to net income,
[01:28 - 01:31] you actually want to multiply by weighted average shares outstanding,
[01:31 - 01:37] because recall that EPS is defined as net income divided by weighted average shares outstanding,
[01:37 - 01:39] not latest shares outstanding.
[01:39 - 01:44] So, the shares that you're multiplying by is different from the numerator and the denominator,
[01:44 - 01:48] and that could lead to a slight difference, usually it's not a material difference,
[01:48 - 01:54] but it could lead to a slight difference between the PE ratio that's calculated using share price DPS,
[01:54 - 01:58] versus the PE ratio that's defined as equity value divided by net income.
[01:58 - 02:05] So, just be aware of what the numerator and the denominator is specifically when you're using the PE ratio,
[02:05 - 02:09] and understand that there is this subtle difference.
[02:09 - 02:15] The difference only becomes material if there are massive changes to the share count during the year.
[02:15 - 02:19] If, for example, during the year, the company went on a massive buyback,
[02:19 - 02:26] where the company issued a lot of shares, those are scenarios in which the PE ratio calculated using the share price DPS approach
[02:26 - 02:30] might be materiality different than equity value, net income approach.
[02:30 - 02:35] So, beyond this sort of granular difference in terms of calculation,
[02:35 - 02:39] let's understand what the point of the PE ratio is,
[02:39 - 02:46] and the point is really that the EPS is used as a proxy for economic equity value.
[02:46 - 02:54] Now, the problem with using EPS as a proxy for value is that EPS is a measure of accounting profit only during a particular period of time.
[02:54 - 02:59] We know from our basic understanding of corporate finance that the value of a business,
[02:59 - 03:03] that the value of a share shouldn't be an exact function of its earnings per share,
[03:03 - 03:06] but it should be a function of all that's expected future cash flows,
[03:06 - 03:11] discounted to the present at some rate that appropriately reflects the riskiness of the cash flows.
[03:11 - 03:14] So, the challenges, of course, EPS is just in a measure of accounting profit.
[03:14 - 03:19] It can be significantly divorced from the cash flows that the business generates,
[03:19 - 03:24] and measures only one particular period of time if you're using last 12 months EPS.
[03:24 - 03:27] That doesn't necessarily mean that future EPS is going to be similar.
[03:27 - 03:30] This year can be very different from EPS next year or the year after that.
[03:30 - 03:34] So, accounting profits can be misleading because they include non-cash and non-recurring items,
[03:34 - 03:37] as well as accounting assumptions that are embedded within EPS,
[03:37 - 03:40] such as historical versus market costing.
[03:40 - 03:43] And this means that EPS can be goose, it can be manipulated.
[03:43 - 03:50] Also, a high-pea valuation relative to Pierce could be justified when high-pea firm has higher growth prospects.
[03:50 - 03:53] So, when you're comparing companies based on a PE ratio and you say,
[03:53 - 03:58] oh, my company has a low PE ratio compared to its peers that are trading at a high-pea ratio.
[03:58 - 04:00] Therefore, my company is undervalued.
[04:00 - 04:04] Well, that may not necessarily be true for slew of reasons.
[04:04 - 04:11] Not the least being that the peer group may have higher growth prospects in which case they would merit a higher-pea valuation.
[04:11 - 04:18] In fact, the underlying value drivers that listed here below is going to help us really understand them.
[04:18 - 04:23] If you parse out what a multiple implies, what drives them multiple.
[04:23 - 04:29] If you really ask, why should one company trade a 10 times PE while another should trade at 40 times PE?
[04:29 - 04:35] The reason is that there are really three variables that should fundamentally drive a multiple.
[04:35 - 04:42] The return on equity, in other words, the higher the company's return on equity, the higher the PE ratio should be.
[04:42 - 04:45] The next variable should be the growth rate.
[04:45 - 04:49] The higher the growth rate, the higher the PE ratio should be.
[04:49 - 04:53] And last but not least, the discount rate.
[04:53 - 04:56] Specifically for equity value multiples, that's the cost of equity.
[04:56 - 04:59] The higher the cost of equity, the lower the multiple should be.
[04:59 - 05:03] Those are the three things that should drive the PE ratio as a multiple, fundamentally.
[05:03 - 05:12] And so, when you're sort of thinking about what's driving the multiple, if you're comparing companies with different ROE prospects, with a different return on equity,
[05:12 - 05:17] with different growth, long-term growth rate expectations, with different costs of capital.
[05:17 - 05:22] Well, those are the things that are going to justifiably lead to differences across PE ratios.
[05:22 - 05:30] A comps analysis only works if you feel like in aggregate, the peers generally have the same fundamental prospects.
[05:30 - 05:32] Only then is it a useful comps analysis.
[05:32 - 05:38] So, if the peer group generally has the same ROE, the same long-term growth rate, the same cost of equity.
[05:38 - 05:44] Then you can say that, oh, my peer group has a higher PE ratio than mine.
[05:44 - 05:50] But since I have similar fundamental drivers, then I can say that maybe my company is undervalued or vice versa.
[05:50 - 05:53] Maybe the peer group is overvalued.
[05:53 - 06:01] But what makes comps particularly challenging, conceptually, is understanding that only if these are all generally the same across the peer group,
[06:01 - 06:06] does the PE ratio become useful for determining whether a company is overvalued or undervalued?
[06:06 - 06:13] The PE ratio is most appropriate for companies that are in a mature phase in their business cycle and their life cycle.
[06:13 - 06:19] And that's because generally those companies tend to have similar growth rates, similar costs of capital.
[06:19 - 06:28] Companies with positive earnings, obviously, a PE ratio for a company with negative earnings per share and negative net income, is going to lead to nonsensical results.
[06:28 - 06:30] Lastly, companies with similar capital structures.
[06:30 - 06:38] The reason why that's important for a PE ratio, and we talked about this in prior slide, is that the denominator is a levered metric.
[06:38 - 06:46] In fact, any time the denominator, in this case, earnings per share and net income, is after the impact of leverage, in other words,
[06:46 - 06:53] after the effect of interest expense or principal payments, you are implicitly accounting for that leverage in the multiple.
[06:53 - 07:01] And therefore, if you want a proper apples to apples comparison, you really should only be comparing against companies with similar leverage.
[07:01 - 07:03] Next, we turn to the PE ratio.
[07:03 - 07:06] The PE ratio is basically a PE ratio divided by the company's long-term growth rate.
[07:06 - 07:14] It solves one of the problems that we address in the prior slide, namely the assumption that the companies that are being compared have the same growth rate.
[07:14 - 07:24] So this is a multiple that you can use to compare companies with different long-term growth rates, but presumably similar returns on equity and cost of equity.
[07:24 - 07:30] Same issues exist in terms of EPS being a poor proxy for cash flows,
[07:30 - 07:33] we're only looking at EPS as of one period at a time.
[07:33 - 07:40] It still suffers from all the accounting issues that we talked about that include non-cash items and non-recurring items, etc.
[07:40 - 07:49] So you can do a lot of things to try to standardize for that, but off the shelf, a peg ratio, just like a PE ratio suffers from a lot of these accounting problems.
[07:49 - 07:53] It's also meaningless for companies where the earnings per share is negative.

### 115. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Convertible-Preferred-Stock

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Convertible-Preferred-Stock/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Convertible-Preferred-Stock.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Convertible-Preferred-Stock\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Convertible-Preferred-Stock.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:01 - 00:04] We've talked about stock options and how that affects the diluted share count.
[00:04 - 00:08] We also need to account for some of the other securities that we talked about, namely, convertible
[00:08 - 00:09] securities.
[00:09 - 00:13] So if the company has convertible preferred stock on its balance sheet, let's start with
[00:13 - 00:15] preferred stock and then we'll address convertible debt.
[00:15 - 00:17] They're basically treated the same way.
[00:17 - 00:20] If a company has convertible preferred stock on its balance sheet, analysts have to determine
[00:20 - 00:25] whether to assume conversion for the purposes of calculating diluted shares outstanding.
[00:25 - 00:30] If conversion is assumed, exclude the preferred stock from the balance sheet and any preferred
[00:30 - 00:34] dividends from the cash flow statement, to determine whether the preferred stock should
[00:34 - 00:37] be assumed converted, we have to employ two tests.
[00:37 - 00:42] First test is are the convertibles in the money or out of the money, okay?
[00:42 - 00:46] If they're in the money, we proceed to test two.
[00:46 - 00:51] And test two basically says, is assuming conversion diluted or anti diluted?
[00:51 - 00:57] If we were to assume conversion, would that lead to dilution or be anti diluted?
[00:57 - 01:00] And if it's dilutive, we assume conversion for the purposes of calculating shares, both
[01:00 - 01:05] of these conditions have to be met and only then can we assume conversion.
[01:05 - 01:08] So let's talk about both those tests and what on earth that means.
[01:08 - 01:12] So the in the money test basically says, if the market share price of a company, so let's
[01:12 - 01:16] take a sample company, let's say we've got Colgate.
[01:16 - 01:23] So let's say Colgate has a share price of $60 per share, just do an example.
[01:23 - 01:27] If Colgate share price is greater than the conversion price, then the convertible
[01:27 - 01:29] preferred stock is in the money.
[01:29 - 01:34] So to determine what the conversion price is, we look at this formula, conversion price
[01:34 - 01:39] equals the liquidation or redemption value for shares divided by the conversion ratio times
[01:39 - 01:41] the preferred shares outstanding.
[01:41 - 01:46] So the numerator here, it represents the value that the firm has to pay to eliminate
[01:46 - 01:51] the obligation, the preferred stock obligation in the event of liquidation or sale.
[01:51 - 01:55] And it's proxied by the book value of the preferred stock on the balance sheet.
[01:55 - 02:02] So let's say for example, that Colgate had at some point issued for $200 million preferred
[02:02 - 02:08] stock, which means the cash went up by $200 million.
[02:08 - 02:11] And now they have this $200 million preferred stock obligation.
[02:11 - 02:16] So you see this cash going up originally when the convertible preferred stock was issued,
[02:16 - 02:20] as well as the preferred stock balance going up on the balance sheet of $200 million.
[02:20 - 02:26] Now the way that preferred stock usually works is you invest as a preferred shareholder
[02:26 - 02:28] $200 million.
[02:28 - 02:30] And it gives you the right to certain dividends down the road.
[02:30 - 02:35] It gives you certain conversion functionality, certain benefits where you can convert
[02:35 - 02:37] into common stock.
[02:37 - 02:43] But it also has one of the core ideas is it has a redemption value.
[02:43 - 02:48] In other words, at any moment, I could redeem this for a certain amount of money.
[02:48 - 02:53] And let's say that at any moment, I could actually get back my $200 million.
[02:53 - 02:54] That's sort of a standard of redemption value.
[02:54 - 02:58] Let's say at any moment, I could redeem this for $200 million.
[02:58 - 03:02] And so that's why we say that the redemption value is often just proxied by the original
[03:02 - 03:03] book value.
[03:03 - 03:08] There's nothing that says that the redemption value has to be the exact amount of money
[03:08 - 03:11] that you originally issued, but it tends to be pretty close.
[03:11 - 03:15] And so either you get from the footnote disclosures, the exact redemption value, or you
[03:15 - 03:17] just proxied using the book value.
[03:17 - 03:19] what we're looking for is the redemption value.
[03:19 - 03:23] How much you can get back for your preferred stock.

[04:29 - 04:30] how these are quoted.

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] In this video, we address one of the more tricky issues that have to do with spreading
[00:06 - 00:07] comps.
[00:07 - 00:12] And that has to do with shares outstanding, specifically, the calculation of diluted shares
[00:12 - 00:17] outstanding, accounting for convertible securities, dealing with things like stock splits,
[00:17 - 00:20] dual classes of shares, let's get started.
[00:20 - 00:24] First of all, you should use diluted shares instead of the actual basic shares when you're
[00:24 - 00:26] calculating market cap.
[00:26 - 00:30] And that's because the market share price accounts for not just the actual shares outstanding,
[00:30 - 00:34] but also potentially dilutive securities, and ignoring that in the share count would
[00:34 - 00:35] understate the market cap of the business.
[00:35 - 00:38] A simple example of help illustrate this point.
[00:38 - 00:41] If you're comparing two pies, the price of the two pies should be identical.
[00:41 - 00:45] But if there's slice in different ways, obviously the observed price for a slice of
[00:45 - 00:48] pizza depends on the size of the slice.
[00:48 - 00:53] So if you've got eight slices for an entire pie that costs $16, it's worth $16, you're
[00:53 - 00:55] going to have the price for each slice be $2.
[00:55 - 00:58] And if it's only four slices, it's going to be worth $4.
[00:58 - 01:03] If you want to calculate the price of the entire pie, like if what's observed, so the
[01:03 - 01:08] issue with stock is that you don't really observe the market cap, which is sort of big
[01:08 - 01:12] proxy by the entire pie here, where you observe as the share price.
[01:12 - 01:19] So if you want to calculate the price of the entire pie implicitly by looking at each slice,
[01:19 - 01:23] the price of each slice, you have to figure out exactly how many slices are in the pie.
[01:23 - 01:28] So if what you're observing is the price per slice, you need to know exactly how many
[01:28 - 01:29] slices we're talking about.
[01:29 - 01:33] And if you get the number of slices wrong, if you're using, say, basic shares instead
[01:33 - 01:37] of diluted shares, you're going to get the price for the entire pie wrong.
[01:37 - 01:39] You're going to get the market cap wrong.
[01:39 - 01:43] It's extremely critical to get the number of shares right.
[01:43 - 01:47] Let's talk about the difference between basic shares and diluted shares.
[01:47 - 01:53] So we've established that the right share count for the market cap is the diluted share
[01:53 - 01:55] count as opposed to the basic share count.
[01:55 - 01:58] Let's talk about what the dilution comes from.
[01:58 - 02:04] So if we defined diluted shares as actual shares, which are the basic share count, plus dilutive
[02:04 - 02:08] securities, which are really shares that aren't quite common stock, but can become common
[02:08 - 02:09] stock.
[02:09 - 02:13] And for the purposes of analysis should be counted as if it's common stock.
[02:13 - 02:17] Well, those diluted securities are stock options primarily.
[02:17 - 02:20] Stock options are issued to pay and motivate employees.
[02:20 - 02:24] It gives the employees the option to purchase common stock at a given price over an
[02:24 - 02:26] extended period of time.
[02:26 - 02:30] Similarly you have something called warrants and from a mechanics purpose and from modeling
[02:30 - 02:33] purposes, they're identical to stock options.
[02:33 - 02:36] The differences in terms of how they're issued, whereas stock options are usually given
[02:36 - 02:42] to employees to motivate them and keep them around, warrants are a sweetener usually to
[02:42 - 02:44] in a debt offering mechanically.
[02:44 - 02:45] The exact same thing.
[02:45 - 02:49] When warrants are exercised, the holder, the lender usually must pay a certain amount
[02:49 - 02:51] of money to obtain the shares.
[02:51 - 02:55] And then when warrants are attached to debt, which is usually the case, the debt remains
[02:55 - 02:58] after the warrants are exercised.
[02:58 - 03:01] Convertible bonds are bonds that the company issues that can be converted into common stock
[03:01 - 03:03] upon a certain strike price.
[03:03 - 03:06] So in this case, the convertible bond no longer is a bond.
[03:06 - 03:11] It becomes common stock unlike warrants, which when they're attached to debt, the debt remains
[03:11 - 03:13] after the warrants are exercised.
[03:13 - 03:14] So this is just an attachment.
[03:14 - 03:18] This is something that converts debt into stock.
[03:18 - 03:22] The conversion feature allows the corporation opportunity to obtain equity capital without
[03:22 - 03:26] giving a more ownership control than necessary or end or entice investors except lower
[03:26 - 03:31] interest rates and they would normally accept on a straight debt issue.
[03:31 - 03:33] Convertible preferred similar convertible debt except that it's preferred stock.
[03:33 - 03:38] So it's stock that usually gives you the right to certain preferred dividends, which
[03:38 - 03:40] you can convert upon certain provisions.
[03:40 - 03:42] But up till now, we sort of taken forgiven.
[03:42 - 03:46] I've just told you, you should use diluted shares instead of basic shares when you calculate
[03:46 - 03:47] market capital.
[03:47 - 03:48] Why?
[03:48 - 03:49] Why is diluted important?
[03:49 - 03:53] So let's go through a couple of exercises that are going to make it hopefully crystal clear
[03:53 - 03:56] why you need diluted.
[03:56 - 04:03] So imagine a business with an equity value $500 million and has $100 million shares outstanding.
[04:03 - 04:07] What would you expect the company share price to be if that's all that existed in terms
[04:07 - 04:08] of capital base?
[04:08 - 04:13] Well at $500 million and $100 million shares outstanding, you're looking at $5 per share.
[04:13 - 04:16] The share count is simply $100 million shares outstanding.
[04:16 - 04:18] That's pretty straightforward.
[04:18 - 04:21] $5 per share.
[04:21 - 04:23] All right.
[04:23 - 04:28] Now assume that option holders hold $25 million exerciseable options and let's assume for
[04:28 - 04:31] simplicity a zero exercise price.
[04:31 - 04:34] In other words, let's say that you can exercise them in any moment.
[04:34 - 04:39] In other words, there are no vesting restrictions and there's no money that the option holders
[04:39 - 04:44] have to give to the company when they exercise each option.
[04:44 - 04:47] It would of course make sense to count these people in the room.
[04:47 - 04:53] There's no downside for them to or limitation for them on exercising these options.
[04:53 - 04:58] And so given that there's basically 25 million additional common shareholders out there,
[04:58 - 05:02] they haven't actually exercised their options, but at any moment, they could.
[05:02 - 05:04] They're exerciseable and there's no skin off their back.
[05:04 - 05:07] They don't have to give you any money upon exercise.
[05:07 - 05:10] So you should definitely consider them in the share count.
[05:10 - 05:13] The share count should therefore be considered $125 million.
[05:13 - 05:19] And so you take the $500 million value of the business divided by the $125 now diluted
[05:19 - 05:22] shares outstanding.
[05:22 - 05:25] And you get that the real value of the company on a per share basis is actually only
[05:25 - 05:27] four per share, four dollars per share.
[05:27 - 05:32] So if you had this information, you knew that there are these option holders.
[05:32 - 05:35] You definitely want to factor that in if you're trying to back into what the right share
[05:35 - 05:42] prices lastly, assume that in addition to the options, convertible preferred shareholders
[05:42 - 05:48] hold 15 million shares, each convertible into five shares of common stock and assume no dividends
[05:48 - 05:51] and no liquidation value.
[05:51 - 05:57] So what that means is you have shareholders that can at any moment convert into 75 million
[05:57 - 06:03] shares of common stock five shares times 15 million shares.
[06:03 - 06:08] There's no reason necessarily for them to not convert, they're not getting any preferred
[06:08 - 06:09] dividends.
[06:09 - 06:10] There's no liquidation value.
[06:10 - 06:16] In fact, the only value we're intentionally making these simplifying assumptions to crystallize
[06:16 - 06:23] the point that these convertible holders and these option holders have no value in not exercising
[06:23 - 06:24] as we'll see in just a moment.
[06:24 - 06:28] There's sometimes where you don't want to assume conversion or dilution from these types
[06:28 - 06:29] of securities.
[06:29 - 06:35] When these convertible securities holders get very high dividends or have a very high liquidation
[06:35 - 06:38] value, in which case maybe they wouldn't want to convert to common stock, but here it's
[06:38 - 06:40] a no brainer.
[06:40 - 06:44] The only value they get out of holding these convertible for preferred is in the conversion
[06:44 - 06:48] feature itself, which has no limitation that we've identified, which means that what we should
[06:48 - 06:56] be thinking about is a share base not of 125, but of 125 plus the 75 or 200.
[06:56 - 07:03] So notice, the none of this has any impact on the numerator, the value of the business,
[07:03 - 07:10] but factoring these security holders in dramatically changes what is the right value per share
[07:10 - 07:12] here.
[07:12 - 07:17] And really, the way that we observe this in the market is we don't observe this price
[07:17 - 07:19] in the market.
[07:19 - 07:23] What's important to understand is what we observe is this price, you know, when you go to
[07:23 - 07:28] Yahoo Finance or Cap IQ or factset and you look at a company's price per share, the
[07:28 - 07:33] market has already factored in all the dilution, because it should, because it actually
[07:33 - 07:38] reflects that there's a whole bunch of value that's going to be split into 200 million
[07:38 - 07:41] pieces, not 100 million pieces.
[07:41 - 07:42] And so this is what you observe.
[07:42 - 07:47] So if you inadvertently go into the fund, when you're spreading a comp or performing a financial
[07:47 - 07:52] analysis and you inadvertently get the share count wrong to get to market cap, you're
[07:52 - 07:56] going to multiply 250 by 100 to back into an applied equity value, you'll get an equity
[07:56 - 07:59] value that's totally understated.
[07:59 - 08:04] It's critical that when you see the observed share price, you multiply by the correct
[08:04 - 08:05] share count.
[08:05 - 08:09] And the correct share count should be diluted shares outstanding when certain conditions
[08:09 - 08:10] are met.
[08:10 - 08:14] So now we've introduced the basic concept, but now let's talk about these conditions.
[08:14 - 08:19] There's a reason why we made this very simple by saying assume a zero exercise price and
[08:19 - 08:24] assume that the options are excisable, assuming no dividends or no liquidation value.
[08:24 - 08:28] Once we relax these assumptions, we're going to see that we have to start employing
[08:28 - 08:32] some tests to determine whether we should count diluted shares in the share count, whether
[08:32 - 08:38] in fact this share price does factor in the conversion of convertible securities or whether
[08:38 - 08:39] the share price does not.

### 118. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Restricted-Stock

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Restricted-Stock/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Restricted-Stock.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Restricted-Stock\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Restricted-Stock.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:07] In addition to stock options, we need to address the treatment of restricted stock in the calculation of diluted shares outstanding.
[00:07 - 00:12] Now, since companies started having to expense stock options on the income statement several years ago,
[00:12 - 00:19] companies have begun to increasingly rely on simply issuing restricted stock instead of stock options as a form of stock based compensation.
[00:19 - 00:23] Now, the question is for us, how do we treat that and that diluted share count?
[00:23 - 00:29] And there are really two things that we have to think about. One is the treatment of restricted shares that have vested.
[00:29 - 00:34] That's pretty easy. Like options, restricted stock vest over several years.
[00:34 - 00:38] But when they vest, they automatically get included in the actual share count.
[00:38 - 00:42] So there's no dilute of impact. It'll simply be part of the basic share count.
[00:42 - 00:46] Now, the question of what do you do with unvested restricted shares?
[00:46 - 00:53] So similarly to unvested options, unvested restricted shares are typically not included in the diluted share count.
[00:53 - 00:55] We basically pretend like they don't exist.
[00:55 - 01:01] Now, that's obviously just from a logical standpoint, maybe not the ideal approach when you have a lot of restricted stock or options for that matter.
[01:01 - 01:04] In an alternative approach, that would be more conceptual.
[01:04 - 01:11] The rigorous might be to apply an illiquidity discount to unvested restricted shares and to include that in the diluted share count.
[01:11 - 01:17] Under the thinking, the employees that are holding these restricted shares haven't passed through their investing restrictions,
[01:17 - 01:21] but they most likely will. A lot of companies have two-year investing periods.
[01:21 - 01:24] So the vast majority of restricted shares end up vesting.
[01:24 - 01:29] And so we should certainly at least consider a portion of those unvested shares in the share count,
[01:29 - 01:34] allowing for some sort of illiquidity discount, allowing for the reality that some of these will be canceled.
[01:34 - 01:40] Now, completely ignoring unvested restricted stock, as most practitioners do, and we will actually do that.
[01:40 - 01:44] We can get away with it when the impacts are somewhat immaterial, but completely ignoring it.
[01:44 - 01:50] When it is material, we'll lead to a potential material underestimation of market cap and enterprise value.
[01:50 - 02:00] And just so we understand what these actual disclosures look like in real life, they're usually the disclosures for restricted stock are usually in the same place as the stock option disclosures,
[02:00 - 02:03] which is the stock based compensation footnote.
[02:03 - 02:10] And here we see F5 networks, which has some restricted stock outstanding. They usually vest quarterly over a two-year period.
[02:10 - 02:18] And so we have the balance of existing unvested restricted stock is 926,000.
[02:18 - 02:30] We are completely going to ignore those unrestricted or unvested restricted shares to be consistent with sort of what is the typical diluted share count calculation approach,
[02:30 - 02:32] but just understand the limitation of that.
[02:32 - 02:36] And the same limitation exists for basically ignoring unvested options.
[02:36 - 02:43] It's not going to be a big deal when the numbers aren't significant when the restricted stock is not a significant percentage of the total share count,
[02:43 - 02:49] but when it is a significant percentage, it may be something worth considering applying an illiquidity discount.

---

### 119. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Shares-Outstanding-Splits--Duel-Classes

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] Next, we turn to stock splits.
[00:03 - 00:08] So when companies announce stock splits, all share count and dilute of securities prior
[00:08 - 00:11] to the split must be adjusted to reflect the split.
[00:11 - 00:15] Otherwise, the market share price will reflect a post-split price while the share count will
[00:15 - 00:20] be pre-split, leading to a huge underestimation of market count.
[00:20 - 00:24] To avoid this, always confirm that no split is taking place subsequent to the latest financial
[00:24 - 00:25] report.
[00:25 - 00:29] If you have access to a Bloomberg terminal, the easiest way to check is to select corporate
[00:29 - 00:34] action or CACS on the Bloomberg terminal to review recent corporate actions, let me
[00:34 - 00:37] just put in plain English what I mean by that.
[00:37 - 00:42] Imagine that you observe a price for a company, a market share price, for a company
[00:42 - 00:49] of $10 a share.
[00:49 - 00:56] In the latest 10Q, you found that they had 500 million shares outstanding.
[00:56 - 01:00] You are going to just multiply those two together to get to a market cap, and you are going
[01:00 - 01:07] going to get to a market cap of $5 billion.
[01:07 - 01:12] You could be grossly mistaken if between the time that you found this disclosure in the
[01:12 - 01:20] 10Q and the date that you looked at to share price, there was a stock split.
[01:20 - 01:22] You'd be really badly mistaken.
[01:22 - 01:28] Imagine there was a two-for-one stock split between the time of the 10Q and the share
[01:28 - 01:29] count.
[01:29 - 01:34] As soon as there was a two-for-one stock split, presumably the share price would have.
[01:34 - 01:41] So if before it was $20 a share, now the sudden it drops down to $10 a share, the market
[01:41 - 01:43] value of this company is not $5 billion.
[01:43 - 01:47] You should be multiplying it by $1 billion shares outstanding.
[01:47 - 01:51] Because this share price reflects now the new split.
[01:51 - 02:00] This would be very incorrect because this is the price that you want to multiply by.
[02:00 - 02:04] Now Apple, so for example, a perfect example is Apple's recent announcement of a seven-for-one
[02:04 - 02:06] stock split.
[02:06 - 02:10] If you were to look at the current share price for Apple after actually their stock split
[02:10 - 02:13] hasn't taken place yet, they're announcing that it's going to take place in a little
[02:13 - 02:14] while.
[02:14 - 02:18] But as soon as that stock split takes place, their share price is going to significantly
[02:18 - 02:19] decline.
[02:19 - 02:21] They're radically by exactly one-seventh.
[02:21 - 02:24] It should be one-seventh of size of its pre-split price.
[02:24 - 02:32] And if you're using the pre-split share count, you're going to grossly miscount the market
[02:32 - 02:33] cap.
[02:33 - 02:35] Always want to double-check.
[02:35 - 02:40] If the company, before you spread a comps analysis to just double-check that there hasn't
[02:40 - 02:44] been a split recently in the easiest ways if you have access to a Bloomberg Terminal to identify
[02:44 - 02:45] corporate actions.
[02:45 - 02:50] Otherwise, you have to look for SEC filings or news about the company.
[02:50 - 02:53] Next thing I want to address are dual classes of shares.
[02:53 - 02:58] So sometimes companies issue two or more classes of common stock, usually it's called
[02:58 - 03:01] class A and class B, where one class has more voting rights.
[03:01 - 03:05] And the rationale is to allow management, families, and other insiders to retain voting
[03:05 - 03:08] control without a one-for-one stake in equity.
[03:08 - 03:11] Both classes equally in the share base include a footnote.
[03:11 - 03:18] So when we do this, in a perfect example is Google, Google and the front cover of every
[03:18 - 03:22] one of their queues and case will make it clear that they have two classes of stock.
[03:22 - 03:26] They have class A and then they have class B.
[03:26 - 03:27] We count both.
[03:27 - 03:34] Both have the same rights to companies' profits, even though they have different voting
[03:34 - 03:35] rights.
[03:35 - 03:40] And they've reserved one class here for owners like Larry and Sergey and other insiders,
[03:40 - 03:43] whereas the other class is reserved for everybody else.

### 120. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-FAQs--Common-Misconceptions.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] We are almost ready to begin the comps model.
[00:03 - 00:06] Before we dive in, I do want to address a few questions
[00:06 - 00:10] that analysts frequently have about the benefits
[00:10 - 00:12] and the shortcomings of a comps model.
[00:12 - 00:14] So the first one is what's a better way to value a business?
[00:14 - 00:16] The DCF are comps.
[00:16 - 00:17] Well, the answer is they're both important.
[00:17 - 00:20] The DCF provides an academically rigorous measure of value,
[00:20 - 00:22] but it's very sensitive to assumptions
[00:22 - 00:24] which makes it easy to manipulate.
[00:24 - 00:26] Trading comps tell you how the market is actually
[00:26 - 00:27] valuable to some of our businesses,
[00:27 - 00:29] still the ones you're analyzing.
[00:29 - 00:32] And so the benefit is that you're getting real world results
[00:32 - 00:35] as opposed to a model derived value.
[00:35 - 00:38] Another way to think about it is that comps provide
[00:38 - 00:41] a market-based sanity check to intrinsic valuation
[00:41 - 00:42] and vice versa.
[00:42 - 00:45] So if you're relying purely on one as opposed to the other,
[00:45 - 00:47] you might have results that don't make sense.
[00:47 - 00:50] So if you're, for example, getting your values purely
[00:50 - 00:53] from a comps analysis without really looking at DCF,
[00:53 - 00:56] whenever the market is wholesale mispricing assets,
[00:56 - 00:58] well, you're not going to get a sanity check
[00:58 - 01:00] to that that's driven by intrinsic cash flows.
[01:00 - 01:04] Conversely, if you're only looking at your DCF model,
[01:04 - 01:08] given the sensitivity of the model to various assumptions,
[01:08 - 01:12] well, there's no market input there to provide a sanity check.
[01:12 - 01:14] So it's really useful to think about them
[01:14 - 01:18] as to interrelated and useful concepts to work together.
[01:18 - 01:21] The other issue is implementation.
[01:21 - 01:22] You can't build a proper DCF model
[01:22 - 01:24] without a lot of detailed financials,
[01:24 - 01:25] but you only need a few data points
[01:26 - 01:28] as we just saw in our simple exercise,
[01:28 - 01:30] like EBITDA sales and that income,
[01:30 - 01:31] to get a comp-based valuation.
[01:31 - 01:34] So it's much easier to value private companies
[01:34 - 01:36] where there's not a lot of access information.
[01:36 - 01:39] If you want to value a private company,
[01:39 - 01:41] all you need to tease out is either EBITDA
[01:41 - 01:43] or top line sales figure,
[01:43 - 01:45] and you can actually arrive at some sort of comps analysis,
[01:45 - 01:48] much more difficult to do a DCF for a company
[01:48 - 01:51] with limited financial disclosures.
[01:51 - 01:56] That said, I will say that ultimately this question
[01:57 - 01:59] is the wrong question to ask,
[01:59 - 02:01] because if you peel the onion a little bit,
[02:01 - 02:05] and you actually revisit the underlying value equations
[02:05 - 02:08] that we discussed in the multiple section earlier,
[02:08 - 02:12] you'd also recognize that applying a peer-derived
[02:12 - 02:14] multiple to value a business
[02:14 - 02:17] is implicitly making a whole bunch of future assumptions
[02:17 - 02:20] about cash flows, cost of capital,
[02:20 - 02:22] and returns on investment capital,
[02:22 - 02:24] that you end up making explicitly
[02:24 - 02:26] when you're building a DCF.
[02:26 - 02:27] They're actually very related.
[02:27 - 02:30] The comps and the DCF are very related concepts
[02:30 - 02:34] in the sense that the DCF explicitly forces the analyst
[02:34 - 02:35] to make assumptions,
[02:35 - 02:38] whereas all of those assumptions exist
[02:38 - 02:41] in the comps analysis, they're just implicit.
[02:41 - 02:42] This is sort of a related question,
[02:42 - 02:46] which is what's the downside to overreliance and comps?
[02:46 - 02:48] Probably the biggest shortcoming of comps
[02:48 - 02:50] is that you're comparing apples and oranges.
[02:50 - 02:52] Truly comparable companies are rare,
[02:52 - 02:54] differences are hard to account for.
[02:54 - 02:57] Despite how much we try to standardize for accounting,
[02:57 - 02:59] to standardize for leverage,
[02:59 - 03:01] to standardize for all the things that we talked about,
[03:01 - 03:04] ultimately standardizing across all of the
[03:04 - 03:06] operational differences, that's difficult to do,
[03:06 - 03:08] which is ultimately like what you're trying to do.
[03:08 - 03:11] What you're trying to do is really compare apples, apples,
[03:11 - 03:13] and that's impossible, virtually impossible with companies.
[03:13 - 03:16] And explaining value gaps between the company
[03:16 - 03:18] and its peer group involves a lot of judgment.
[03:19 - 03:21] The other issue is thinly traded small caps
[03:21 - 03:23] or poorly followed stocks may not really reflect
[03:23 - 03:24] on a mental value.
[03:25 - 03:28] Lastly, many people feel that the stock market
[03:28 - 03:32] does not efficiently represent value
[03:32 - 03:35] and has irrational fluctuations.
[03:35 - 03:37] Market can be wrong, and therefore using it
[03:37 - 03:41] to value a company suffers from the mistake
[03:41 - 03:45] that the entire peer group is also mispriced.
[03:45 - 03:48] Now, the sort of flip side to that is if you subscribe
[03:48 - 03:50] to the idea that the market is what matters
[03:50 - 03:52] when valuing a public company,
[03:52 - 03:54] do you even need a comps analysis?
[03:54 - 03:56] What's the point of a comps analysis
[03:56 - 03:58] when you're valuing a public company?
[03:58 - 03:59] Why not just use the market cap
[03:59 - 04:01] of the company directly to value it?
[04:01 - 04:04] If the market is, if we're using the market
[04:04 - 04:06] to value the business,
[04:06 - 04:09] the implicit assumption has to be that we believe
[04:09 - 04:11] we have faith in the market.
[04:11 - 04:13] Well, why not just use the market's valuation
[04:13 - 04:15] of the company itself?
[04:15 - 04:16] Now, you can't really do that with private companies,
[04:16 - 04:18] with public companies.
[04:18 - 04:19] Like in our example, Colgate,
[04:19 - 04:22] why not just use the Colgate Share price itself
[04:22 - 04:22] to value Colgate?
[04:22 - 04:24] If the market's right, the Colgate Share price
[04:24 - 04:26] should be $30.
[04:26 - 04:27] In fact, this is such an important concept.
[04:27 - 04:29] Let's go back and revisit and make sure we understand
[04:29 - 04:34] the fundamental issue that raises that question,
[04:34 - 04:37] which is why do I need to go through,
[04:37 - 04:39] why do I need to look at Kimberly Clark
[04:39 - 04:42] and all these other companies in the peer group
[04:42 - 04:45] to back into what the implied Colgate Share prices?
[04:45 - 04:46] If the market's right,
[04:46 - 04:48] if I'm using the market-derived multiples
[04:48 - 04:51] to get at the value of Colgate,
[04:51 - 04:53] why do I need to get an implied Colgate Share price?
[04:53 - 04:56] Why shouldn't I just look at the actual share price?
[04:56 - 04:57] The Colgate hasn't said,
[04:57 - 05:00] well, this is how the market is value in Colgate.
[05:00 - 05:01] What's wrong with this?
[05:02 - 05:05] And the answer is that the idea underlying
[05:05 - 05:07] a comps analysis for a public company
[05:07 - 05:10] is that the market may be efficient on average,
[05:10 - 05:14] but it can be off when pricing individual companies.
[05:15 - 05:17] And so that's a really interesting point
[05:17 - 05:19] and it's by no means intuitive.
[05:19 - 05:21] The basic idea that makes a comps analysis
[05:21 - 05:23] useful for a public company
[05:23 - 05:26] is only if you believe that the market
[05:26 - 05:30] is efficiently prices securities on mass,
[05:30 - 05:33] but not in terms of individual pricing.
[05:34 - 05:37] Because otherwise, if the market was perfectly efficient
[05:37 - 05:39] and stands the reason that it would price
[05:39 - 05:41] the individual equity correctly,
[05:41 - 05:42] rendering a comps analysis of pointless.
[05:44 - 05:46] When using comps to value a public company,
[05:46 - 05:48] should I include the company in the peer group?
[05:48 - 05:52] This is another debated topic with implementation of comps.
[05:52 - 05:54] Some professionals actually prefer to exclude
[05:54 - 05:57] the target of the analysis from the peer group
[05:57 - 05:58] because it will skew the multiple
[05:58 - 06:01] towards the target companies' actual trading values,
[06:01 - 06:05] assuming, of course, that the company is a public company.
[06:05 - 06:06] This question really only applies
[06:06 - 06:08] to when you're valuing a public company
[06:08 - 06:10] because then there's an observable market value
[06:10 - 06:12] and you have observable multiples.
[06:13 - 06:16] Despite this preference,
[06:16 - 06:18] logic actually dictates that the target
[06:18 - 06:21] should be included when it's public.
[06:21 - 06:23] If the intuition behind, and here's the reason,
[06:23 - 06:25] if the intuition behind a comps analysis
[06:25 - 06:28] is that the market may miss price individual stocks
[06:28 - 06:29] but is correct on the whole,
[06:29 - 06:31] well, clearly then the target company
[06:31 - 06:34] should be included in its own market-based valuation.
[06:34 - 06:36] It should be part of the mosaic
[06:36 - 06:40] that represents the efficient sort of market pricing
[06:40 - 06:42] of a group of securities.
[06:42 - 06:44] Next question is, should I use a median or mean
[06:44 - 06:46] calculating the comps derived multiples?
[06:46 - 06:48] For larger peer groups,
[06:48 - 06:50] calculating relevant peer group statistics
[06:50 - 06:52] using the median is preferable to the mean
[06:52 - 06:55] because it limits distortions from outliers.
[06:55 - 06:56] For smaller peer groups,
[06:56 - 06:58] like less than five companies,
[06:58 - 07:04] with no outliers, a mean is preferable.
[07:04 - 07:06] Next question is, should I place more weight
[07:06 - 07:09] on LTM or forward multiples?
[07:09 - 07:10] And the answer to that is it completely depends.
[07:10 - 07:13] And it specifically depends on the situation.
[07:13 - 07:15] Using historical LTM profits is nice
[07:15 - 07:18] because it represents actual burden-hand results.
[07:18 - 07:19] And forecast for smaller cap companies
[07:19 - 07:21] can be hard to come by.
[07:21 - 07:23] However, LTM suffers from the problem
[07:23 - 07:26] that the market values companies off of future
[07:26 - 07:28] not historical profits and cash flows.
[07:28 - 07:31] And if LTM results do not include a lot of
[07:31 - 07:34] non-recurring items and no significant margin
[07:34 - 07:36] and growth changes are expected over the next few years,
[07:36 - 07:39] the LTM results can and should be used side by side
[07:39 - 07:40] with forward multiples.
[07:40 - 07:42] But if these conditions are not met,
[07:42 - 07:44] and LTM results have a whole lot of noise,
[07:44 - 07:46] you should place more weight on forward multiples.

### 121. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-Overview

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-Overview/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-Overview.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-Overview\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Review-Trading-Comps-Overview.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] We've already touched on this a little bit in the prior slide, so let's discuss this in a little bit more detail now.
[00:05 - 00:11] In addition to the need to standardize for size differences, other non-operational differences should be taken into account
[00:11 - 00:17] so as to not distort comparisons when you're value in companies by using other companies as comps.
[00:17 - 00:21] Probably the biggest thing to standardize in addition to size is leverage.
[00:21 - 00:25] And we saw this with the equity value multiples versus the enterprise value multiples.
[00:25 - 00:31] Specifically, the enterprise value numerator is a measure of value independent of leverage.
[00:31 - 00:39] Revenue, EBITDA, and EBIT as well as unlevered free cash flow denominators provide a measure of profit that's independent of leverage.
[00:39 - 00:41] It doesn't matter how much debt the company's taken on.
[00:41 - 00:46] The four metrics that we just addressed are not going to be any different depending on the leverage.
[00:46 - 00:50] There are before interest, there are before principal payments.
[00:50 - 00:57] In addition to financial leverage differences, we also may want to consider standardizing across different accounting differences.
[00:57 - 01:01] Things like depreciation method or useful life assumptions.
[01:01 - 01:08] So if we use profit metrics before depreciation and amortization like revenue or EBITDA or unlevered free cash flows,
[01:08 - 01:12] we are standardizing for accounting differences.
[01:12 - 01:15] In other words, we're ignoring accounting differences.
[01:15 - 01:21] Imagine two identical companies now keep in mind two identical companies should theoretically have identical multiples.
[01:21 - 01:23] But what if there was a difference?
[01:23 - 01:28] It's simply an accounting difference that has nothing to do with impact on cash or taxes.
[01:28 - 01:30] It's just a simple accounting difference.
[01:30 - 01:37] Specifically, one with a useful life assumption for all of its assets within average of 20 years.
[01:37 - 01:43] And the other company, for the exact same assets, chose to have a useful life assumption of 15 years.
[01:43 - 01:47] Well, if you look at EBIT for those companies, it's going to be different across the two companies.
[01:47 - 01:49] Whereas enterprise value is going to be the same.
[01:49 - 01:56] So you're going to have a higher multiple for the company with the higher depreciation expense or the lower useful life assumption.
[01:56 - 01:57] That shouldn't be the case.
[01:57 - 01:59] You want to look at two identical companies.
[01:59 - 02:01] They should have the same exact multiple presumably.
[02:01 - 02:08] Well, if you move up the income statement and you look at something like EBITDA, some of the major accounting differences
[02:08 - 02:14] differences that you see across companies like the depreciation method approach that you choose whether you strain line are accelerated
[02:14 - 02:16] or what useful life assumption you're going to make.
[02:16 - 02:23] You're going to be able to ignore that if you go up the income statement instead of you focusing on earnings
[02:23 - 02:27] or earnings before interest in taxes all the way up to EBITDA or revenue.
[02:27 - 02:31] Now, standardizing for this, so looking at EBITDA as opposed to EBIT,
[02:31 - 02:35] is going to be useful for companies with comparable levels of capital intensity.
[02:35 - 02:36] Otherwise, it's dangerous.
[02:36 - 02:45] Imagine two companies, one is extremely capital intensive and the other one isn't and you are basically ignoring the capital charge of depreciation expense
[02:45 - 02:50] or the accrual methods way to look at capital charges, depreciation expense.
[02:50 - 03:01] If you're going to ignore that, you are going to start getting skewed results because you're not recognizing the expense associated with a company being capital intensive.
[03:01 - 03:06] Next, we turn to temporary distortions, primarily we're talking about non-recurring items.
[03:06 - 03:10] When you're using multiples, they use historical profits as the denominator.
[03:10 - 03:20] Those profits have to be scrubbed to exclude distorted one-time items such as restructuring expenses, litigation costs and one-time gains on sale.
[03:20 - 03:27] Other accounting differences, like differences between how companies classify leases, whether one company classifies a leases,
[03:27 - 03:37] capital lease, and the other may classify that same leases and operating lease, that's going to have a material difference potentially when the companies have a lot of leases on what the financials look like.
[03:37 - 03:46] Similarly, life-overses, FIFO decisions for inventory is going to make a difference in terms of what cost of goods sold is identified on the income statement.
[03:46 - 03:54] Whether these issues rise to the level of having to be dealt with depends on the industry and the specific companies in question.
[03:54 - 04:08] Companies that have a lot of inventory and the difference between life-o and inventory is going to be significant, that's going to be something where it makes sense to standardize all companies to one inventory approach so that you can actually compare on an apples to apples basis.
[04:08 - 04:15] And the same goes for leases. Companies that don't have a lot of leases, you're not going to have to really worry about this lease classification adjustment.
[04:15 - 04:19] Companies with a lot of leases, that's something you need to start worrying about.
[04:19 - 04:28] Lastly, business life cycle differences. So comparable companies operationally may be at different phases of their life cycle, early stage versus growth versus maturity versus decline.
[04:28 - 04:35] This is somewhat of a art as opposed to science, determining where a company is in its business cycle.
[04:35 - 04:48] But companies that have high growth expectations, in other words, that they're at an early stage versus low growth, even if they're in the same line of business, you may want to consider standardizing against their long-term growth rate expectations.
[04:48 - 04:55] So multiples like the peg ratio really standardizes against different long-term growth rate expectations.
[04:55 - 05:02] While other multiples like EV to revenue or EV to EBITDA facilitate comparisons for early stage companies which are generating losses.
[05:02 - 05:16] So there are ways in which you can deal with not only companies at early stages that aren't generating profits, but even inter business life cycle comparisons are doable if you standardize against the different growth rate expectations.

[07:42 - 07:58] That's when in fact you observe in the market with company with public companies and that's in fact the case with private companies as well that the value of their business is not simply worth the the summation of all the book values that represent the historical values of the assets that they purchased in the past.
