[08:06 - 08:12] That's simply the share price times the shares outstanding we call that the market cap or the market capitalization.
[08:12 - 08:18] And if you look at Google for example and compare its book value to its market value you can see a massive difference.
[08:18 - 08:22] Google has an equity book value of 75 billion dollars.
[08:22 - 08:31] Now that's the book value that represents the value of equity if you consider only the book value of the assets and the book value of the liabilities.
[08:31 - 08:35] If you look at the market Google's shares are trading at eight hundred and seventy five dollars a share.
[08:35 - 08:43] And they have three hundred and thirty seven million shares outstanding which implies a market value or a market cap of two hundred ninety billion dollars.
[08:43 - 08:50] Compare to ninety billion to seventy five billion the market cap here is much bigger than the book value would suggest.
[08:50 - 09:16] Google has fifty billion dollars in cash five billion in debt per the company's latest queue implying a enterprise value of two ninety minus forty five billion that the enterprise value the value of its operating assets let its liabilities is two hundred forty five billion here we clearly see a disconnect between the market values and the book value.
[09:16 - 09:25] Before we talk about trading comp specifically as a valuation method let's sort of put an umbrella around it how does it fit within the overall valuation umbrella.
[09:25 - 09:32] First we have to address the two pillars of valuation relative valuation versus DCF valuation.
[09:32 - 09:41] So while intrinsic valuation or DCF valuation is derived from the fundamental analysis of a company's cash flow generation potential.
[09:41 - 09:51] Relative valuation or comps is derived by simply comparing the company to its comparable peers and essentially looking at the value of its peer group.
[09:51 - 09:58] And saying well if the peer group is valued at X amount that is a valuation that makes sense for this company.
[09:58 - 10:07] So why we use trading comps to value companies we do this to determine what the appropriate value of the company is based on the market values of operationally similar companies.
[10:07 - 10:13] If you think about looking at the value of a house when you're actually trying to figure out what is the value of your house.
[10:13 - 10:18] You do that by looking at the the values of similar houses in the neighborhood that's a comps analysis.
[10:18 - 10:28] Now the challenge with companies is opposed to houses is that they're trickier to value because you can't ever really find a truly comparable company.
[10:28 - 10:30] No two companies are truly alike.
[10:30 - 10:40] Whereas houses you look at two houses in the neighborhood have the same square footage built in the same way, have access to the same school system, the same tenant base.
[10:40 - 10:42] Those are virtually identical types of assets.
[10:42 - 10:44] Whereas with companies it's a little more difficult.
[10:44 - 10:51] So what you end up doing is standardizing instead of looking at absolute values and just comparing absolute values the way you look at with houses.
[10:51 - 10:54] You standardize across different factors.
[10:54 - 10:59] So you may have two companies that are very similar except that one is much bigger than the other.
[10:59 - 11:02] You don't compare the absolute values but what you do is you look at multiples.
[11:02 - 11:13] You standardize against various measures of a firm's profitability and some of the most common ways to standardize or to compare is to use multiples like enterprise value to eBITDA multiple.
[11:13 - 11:24] Where you're comparing, you're not comparing absolute enterprise values but you're benchmarking them against the peer groups, the individual companies eBITDA ratios.
[11:24 - 11:31] So what you're comparing are multiples of eBITDA or multiples of revenue or multiples of eBIT.
[11:31 - 11:37] These three types are called enterprise value multiples because the numerator is enterprise value.
[11:37 - 11:41] Whereas on the other side you have equity value multiples.
[11:41 - 11:47] PE ratios, the company's share price divided by its earnings per share or the market cap divided by net income.
[11:47 - 11:49] These two are virtually identical.
[11:49 - 11:56] There are slight granular differences between using share price divided by EPS versus market cap by net income.
[11:56 - 11:59] And we'll talk about those later in the comps analysis.
[11:59 - 12:00] And then you have PE growth.
[12:00 - 12:02] That's another equity value multiple.
[12:02 - 12:10] And really the big picture differences between these two approaches is that enterprise value multiples really don't take into account any leverage differences across the company.
[12:10 - 12:12] The companies that are being compared.
[12:12 - 12:15] Imagine you're comparing your house against other houses.
[12:15 - 12:20] You're not really considering what the mortgages on any of these houses.
[12:20 - 12:28] The leverage issue isn't usually analyzed when you're thinking about buying a house because you're going to whatever asset you're going to buy.
[12:28 - 12:30] You're going to leverage your own rate.
[12:30 - 12:34] And so the idea here is that these types of multiples really don't consider leverage.
[12:34 - 12:39] Whereas on the equity multiple side, you're taking leverage into consideration.
[12:39 - 12:50] You're saying what is a PE multiple, for example, measures not only the company's operational performance in comparison across the peer group, but really also factors in things like leverage.
[12:50 - 13:00] And so a highly levered company that might be operationally identical to an unlevered company are going to have very different PE ratios.
[13:00 - 13:05] Whereas if you did that example on an enterprise value, you bet that basis.
[13:05 - 13:11] An unlevered company that's operationally identical to a levered company is going to have the exact same enterprise value you bet that mold.

### 123. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Simple-Transaction-Comps-Exercise

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Simple-Transaction-Comps-Exercise/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Simple-Transaction-Comps-Exercise.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Simple-Transaction-Comps-Exercise\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Simple-Transaction-Comps-Exercise.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:09] The sequence of steps required to complete a transaction comps analysis are nearly identical to those required to complete a trading comps analysis.
[00:09 - 00:12] First, we start with selecting a peer group.
[00:12 - 00:16] In this case, we need to determine the universe of comparable transactions.
[00:16 - 00:26] What makes transaction comps slightly more challenging than trading comps is that finding those comparable transactions can be even harder than finding comparable companies.
[00:26 - 00:37] One of the things that we're going to do throughout this course is how to perform proper screens for comparable transactions and how to filter out transactions that are truly comparable in those that aren't.
[00:37 - 00:46] The next step, very much like when you spread trading comps, is to calculate and use relevant multiples from those comparable transactions.
[00:46 - 00:57] Specifically, you use offer price to EPS multiples, which is the equivalent to a PE ratio offer value to book value.
[00:57 - 01:04] This is the equivalent of price to book ratios, especially useful when you're doing a transaction comps analysis for banks.
[01:04 - 01:14] Again, that's the equivalent of EV to EBITDA, TV to EBITDA revenue. Those are probably the three most common enterprise value or transaction value multiples.
[01:14 - 01:16] We use those terms interchangeably.
[01:16 - 01:20] And then lastly, we have industry specific enterprise and equity multiples.
[01:20 - 01:26] Real estate, oil and gas, retail, these are all industries that have some specific nuances potentially.
[01:26 - 01:33] We spend a lot of time talking in the prior section about which multiples are specific to which industries.
[01:33 - 01:49] We use those multiples. We apply the mean or the median of those multiples and apply those mean or median multiples to the corresponding operating metric of the actual target that we're looking at to arrive at a potential value.
[01:49 - 01:59] So, at this point, why don't we complete a mini transaction comps exercise, very similar to the mini trading comps exercise that we had built in the trading comps course.
[01:59 - 02:05] So, here we have Eli Lilly. The share price is $50 per share. They have a billion shares outstanding.
[02:05 - 02:12] Revenue is around $20 billion. They have EV to $6.5 billion with net income of $5 billion in net debt of $1 billion.
[02:12 - 02:17] Now, we have located, we've done the heavy lifting for you. In this case, and we've found four comparable transactions.
[02:17 - 02:25] We're trying to figure out what is the potential value of Eli Lilly if we look at it from the perspective of if Eli Lilly was to get acquired by somebody.
[02:25 - 02:34] What types of multiples would they expect to get in that type of transaction and based on that are the shares currently overvalued or undervalued?

[10:42 - 10:44] If you look at the balance, it looked like they did.
[10:44 - 10:48] This affiliate debt looks like they've
[10:48 - 10:52] got affiliate debt of $94.875 million.
[10:52 - 10:55] Well, if you look at that same exhibit $99.3,
[10:55 - 10:58] they actually tell you a little bit about that affiliate debt.
[10:58 - 10:59] Here's that disclosure.
[10:59 - 11:04] It's on page seven of exhibit $99.3.
[11:04 - 11:07] And terraces was formerly a wholly owned subsidiary
[11:07 - 11:08] of some other company, which is interned
[11:08 - 11:11] formed by a joint venture transaction.
[11:11 - 11:13] Historical and terraces financial statements
[11:13 - 11:16] include significant related party transactions
[11:16 - 11:18] with the JV.
[11:18 - 11:20] And these have been eliminated as the company
[11:20 - 11:23] will have no affiliation with the former owners.
[11:23 - 11:25] The pro-former adjustments reflect the elimination
[11:25 - 11:27] of such related party transactions
[11:27 - 11:29] from the historical financial statements,
[11:29 - 11:33] including affiliate receivables, notes receivable, affiliate
[11:33 - 11:35] payables, affiliate debt, and related interest expense.
[11:35 - 11:37] And that's a very important disclosure,
[11:37 - 11:40] because what it's saying is that the debt
[11:40 - 11:44] and the corresponding assets associated with that liability
[11:44 - 11:47] are effectively an intercompany elimination
[11:47 - 11:51] that has nothing to do with what extreme networks
[11:51 - 11:52] is going to receive.
[11:52 - 11:55] When they acquired and terraces,
[11:55 - 11:58] they didn't get the benefits of those receivables.
[11:58 - 12:00] Nor did they get the liability associated
[12:00 - 12:01] with that affiliate debt.
[12:01 - 12:03] Those are intercompany effectively
[12:03 - 12:07] eliminations between the JV and and terraces
[12:07 - 12:09] before the deal was done.
[12:09 - 12:11] But the way this effect is is when we get to the net debt
[12:11 - 12:14] calculation, presumably the fact that they
[12:14 - 12:16] don't get these receivables, the asset side,
[12:16 - 12:19] is reflected via a lower purchase price, right?
[12:19 - 12:21] All else equal, the more receivables, the more assets
[12:21 - 12:22] you get, the higher the purchase price.
[12:22 - 12:24] Since they presumably from the footnote disclosures
[12:24 - 12:26] aren't getting those receivables,
[12:26 - 12:28] they would if they're giving a lower purchase price
[12:28 - 12:29] and had they received it.
[12:29 - 12:33] But correspondingly, you don't want to count the liability
[12:33 - 12:37] as something that now extreme networks has to deal with.
[12:37 - 12:41] So the transaction value is actually not higher
[12:41 - 12:44] than the equity value, the transaction value,
[12:44 - 12:46] the value of the operations is actually lower
[12:46 - 12:48] than the equity value.
[12:48 - 12:50] And that's because there's no debt
[12:50 - 12:53] that this company brings to the table if you acquire it.
[12:53 - 12:54] So we get a zero that out.
[12:54 - 12:56] You could have completely ignored that
[12:56 - 12:57] and you would have been off significantly
[12:57 - 12:58] into the transaction value.
[12:58 - 13:00] You would be surprised at how often these kinds
[13:00 - 13:04] of mistakes are made because it's so easy to miss that
[13:04 - 13:05] if you don't read the financial statements.
[13:05 - 13:08] So it's very important to understand what you're doing.
[13:08 - 13:10] If you get any sort of alarm bells,
[13:10 - 13:12] like when you look at something called affiliate debt
[13:12 - 13:14] and you say, wait, what is that affiliate debt?
[13:14 - 13:16] Do the digging to try to understand what that's all about.
[13:17 - 13:21] All right, at this point we spread the comp
[13:21 - 13:26] and it's time to try to find forecasts.
[13:26 - 13:28] And with projections, again, we encounter a problem
[13:28 - 13:31] because in terraces is a private company
[13:31 - 13:33] when it's acquired.
[13:33 - 13:35] There is no proxy filed.
[13:35 - 13:40] There are no equity research forecasts available.
[13:40 - 13:43] And we found no forecasts in any filings

### 126. Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Synergies--Premiums

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_TC/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Synergies--Premiums/transcript/Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Synergies--Premiums.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_TC\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Synergies--Premiums\transcript\Transaction-Comps-Modeling-Synergies--Premiums.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] Before we can start spreading actual transaction comps, I want to address specifically two things that appear in a transaction comps analysis
[00:06 - 00:10] that don't appear in a trading comps analysis. The first are premiums.
[00:10 - 00:16] So unlike trading comps, transaction comps provide insight into the actual multiples that acquire can expect to have to pay.
[00:16 - 00:23] And that means that deal multiples reflect the premiums that acquire pay to gain control of the target. Often these premiums are an
[00:23 - 00:31] excess of 25% above the standalone market values. And here I've shown you a chart that shows premiums by deal volume.
[00:31 - 00:40] So specifically, premiums of 10% to 25% account for 19% of the total volume.
[00:40 - 00:49] Premiums of 25% to 50% account for 34% of the volume and then 50% to 75% account for 42% of the volume.
[00:49 - 00:56] This is in Q2 of 2014. So we're seeing pretty high premiums right now. Of course, multiples and premiums are sensitive to the current M&A environment.
[00:56 - 01:02] So looking at a three year old deal may prove stale in terms of the kinds of premiums and kinds of multiples you can expect to achieve.
[01:02 - 01:06] And this is sort of one of the main challenges with transaction comps.
[01:06 - 01:13] And that is you're trying to understand what are reasonable multiples for a company that might get acquired.
[01:13 - 01:22] But it's incorporating premiums that are pretty environment specific and you don't always have very good comparable deals over the last year or two.
[01:22 - 01:28] You often end up going back five, six, even seven years to get comps. So that's one of the main challenges you have to deal with.
[01:28 - 01:32] So premiums are also affected by the potential synergies that the deal can generate.
[01:32 - 01:41] For example, a strategic acquire may pay a higher premium than a financial buyer like a private equity firm because of the ability to extract synergies from the deal.
[01:41 - 01:47] And as a result, synergies can also be compared across past transactions to add context to the analysis.
[01:47 - 01:56] All else equal, if you've got to deal with a significant amount of synergies as a percentage of the of EBITDA, you're going to probably see higher premiums.
[01:56 - 02:01] Okay, when ready move on to the next video and we're going to start talking about the actual process of spreading comps.

---
